Vous avez dit&nbsp;:
Below is a one-page Adaptive Feedback Sheet designed for on-site use with students during your intervention. It integrates the three analytical phases of your study:
Writing performance analysis
Learning analytics behavioural indicators
Adaptive instructional feedback generation
This instrument operationalizes the adaptive assessment cycle required in your thesis: diagnosis → analytics interpretation → targeted feedback. In learning analytics literature, such tools are used to transform raw learner data into actionable pedagogical decisions, a principle emphasized by George Siemens.
Adaptive Blended Assessment Feedback Sheet
(Used during the writing intervention session)
Student ID: ________
Date: ________
Writing Task: ___________________
Phase 1 — Writing Performance Diagnosis
Evaluate the essay using the analytic rubric.
Writing Variable	Score (1–5)	Diagnostic Notes
Grammar Accuracy
Lexical Range
Text Organization
Cohesion Devices
Argumentation Quality
Overall Writing Score = Average of all categories: _______
Interpretation:
4–5 → strong competence
3 → moderate performance
1–2 → area requiring adaptive support
Analytic scoring enables the identification of specific linguistic and rhetorical weaknesses, which is fundamental for diagnostic writing assessment according to Sara Cushing Weigle.
Phase 2 — Learning Analytics Behaviour Indicators
Extract behavioural data from Moodle.
Indicator	Observed Value	Interpretation
Platform Logins
Resource Access
Draft Submissions
Revision Frequency
Feedback Engagement	High / Medium / Low
Engagement interpretation:
High engagement → active learning behaviour
Moderate engagement → partial participation
Low engagement → potential self-regulation difficulty
Behavioural indicators provide insight into learners’ participation patterns and self-regulated learning processes.
Phase 3 — Adaptive Instructional Feedback
Using the results of Phase 1 and Phase 2, provide targeted adaptive feedback.
A. Performance-Based Feedback
Identified Weakness	Adaptive Feedback Provided
Grammar ≤ 2	explanation of grammar rule + focused practice
Cohesion ≤ 2	modelling of connectors and transitions
Organization ≤ 2	paragraph structure scaffold
Argumentation ≤ 2	claim–evidence structure guidance
B. Engagement-Based Feedback
Behavioural Pattern	Instructor Response
Low platform access	motivational reminder and task clarification
Limited resource consultation	recommend specific materials
No draft submission	enforce staged drafting
C. Revision-Based Feedback
Revision Pattern	Intervention
No revision	guided revision checklist
Surface corrections only	encourage meaning-level revision
Active revision	provide advanced writing challenge
Process-oriented revision is widely recognized as a key mechanism in writing development within second language pedagogy (Hyland).
Final Adaptive Feedback Delivered to the Student
Strengths identified:
Areas for improvement:
Specific adaptive tasks assigned:
Next writing goal:
How This Tool Supports Your Experimental Design
This sheet operationalizes the adaptive assessment mechanism of your study.
During each session:
The student submits or writes an essay.
The instructor analyses the text using the analytic rubric.
Moodle behavioural data are consulted.
The instructor generates personalized adaptive feedback using the rule framework.
Students revise their writing accordingly.
Thus, the sheet becomes the practical interface between learning analytics and adaptive pedagogy.
Key Academic Sources
Weigle, S. C. (2002). Assessing Writing. Cambridge University Press.
https://doi.org/10.1017/CBO9780511732997
Hyland, K. (2019). Second Language Writing. Cambridge University Press.
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781108635547
Siemens, G. (2013). Learning analytics: The emergence of a discipline. American Behavioral Scientist.
https://doi.org/10.1177/0002764213498851

Keep all completed sheets. They will serve as qualitative evidence of the adaptive intervention, complementing your statistical analysis (pre-test vs post-test).
If you want, I can also show you the strongest methodological element for your thesis defense:
the Adaptive Feedback Algorithm Table (a clear decision tree that examiners love because it shows exactly how analytics produce adaptation).
2. Thesis Title
Adaptive Blended Assessment Through Learning Analytics and Artificial Intelligence to Enhance Academic Writing: A Case Study of Third-Year EFL Students at Belhadj Bouchaib University
3. Research Problem
Many EFL students struggle with academic writing, particularly in higher education contexts where they are expected to produce structured argumentative or expository texts.
Traditional assessment methods are often:
static
summative
not personalized
As a result, they fail to provide adaptive feedback based on learners’ behaviour and performance data.
Recent developments in learning analytics, artificial intelligence, and Bayesian modelling offer opportunities to design adaptive blended assessment systems capable of generating real-time pedagogical feedback.
Learning analytics can track students’ behavioural data in learning management systems, while Bayesian approaches can predict learning outcomes and update probability models based on new evidence.
4. Research Objectives
To design an adaptive blended assessment framework integrating classroom instruction with Moodle-based digital assessment.
To collect and analyse learning analytics data from students’ interactions with the Moodle platform.
To apply Artificial Intelligence techniques to analyse student writing and engagement patterns.
To implement Bayesian statistical modelling to predict student performance and update feedback mechanisms dynamically.
To provide adaptive feedback that supports the development of academic writing competence among EFL learners.
5. Conceptual Framework
The research integrates four theoretical domains:
1. Blended Learning
Combination of face-to-face instruction and online learning environments.
Reference:
Charles R. Graham
Graham defines blended learning as the systematic integration of online and face-to-face instructional modalities.
Source
https://doi.org/10.4324/9780203876084
2. Learning Analytics
Learning analytics refers to the measurement, collection, analysis, and reporting of data about learners and their contexts in order to optimize learning.
Reference
George Siemens
Dragan Gašević
Source
https://doi.org/10.1145/2330601.2330605
3. Artificial Intelligence in Education
Artificial Intelligence enables automated analysis of:
learner behaviour
writing patterns
feedback generation
Reference
Wayne Holmes
Source
https://doi.org/10.1787/9789264303012-en
4. Bayesian Modelling in Learning Analytics
Bayesian statistics allows probabilistic modelling of learning processes, enabling systems to update predictions when new student data becomes available.
Reference
Kevin P. Murphy
Source
https://probml.github.io/pml-book/
Another key work:
Ryan S. J. d. Baker
Source
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4614-3305-7

The Bayesian approach allows the system to update predictions about student learning progress.

Where behaviour data includes:

The model dynamically updates when new evidence is observed.
Advantages:
handles uncertainty

enables personalized feedback

The study aims to contribute to EFL didactics by proposing a model of:
AI-supported adaptive blended assessment using Bayesian learning analytics.

identify struggling writers early
personalize feedback
improve academic writing outcomes
support data-informed pedagogy.

Your current outline already demonstrates strong conceptual ambition, but to make it methodologically defensible at the doctoral level, the elements must be articulated with clearer research logic, operational variables, and analytical alignment. Below is a refined and academically strengthened version of your framework that examiners typically expect in a doctoral dissertation in EFL didactics and educational technology.
Refined Research Framework for the Dissertation
1. Thesis Title
Adaptive Blended Assessment Through Learning Analytics and Artificial Intelligence to Enhance Academic Writing: A Case Study of Third-Year EFL Students at Belhadj Bouchaib University
This title appropriately integrates the pedagogical objective (academic writing development) with the technological mechanisms (learning analytics, artificial intelligence, and adaptive assessment).
2. Research Problem
Despite several decades of research on second language writing pedagogy, many university-level EFL learners continue to experience significant difficulties in producing coherent and academically structured texts. These difficulties are particularly visible in argumentative and expository writing tasks that require advanced discourse organization, lexical sophistication, and critical reasoning.
One of the central challenges lies in the limitations of traditional assessment practices. In many higher education contexts, writing assessment remains predominantly:
summative rather than formative
static rather than adaptive
teacher-centered rather than data-informed
Consequently, students often receive delayed and generalized feedback that does not adequately address their individual learning needs.
Recent advances in learning analytics and artificial intelligence provide new opportunities for transforming assessment into a dynamic and adaptive pedagogical process. Learning analytics enables the systematic collection and analysis of behavioural data generated by students in digital learning environments, while artificial intelligence techniques can support automated analysis of linguistic patterns and learner performance.
Moreover, Bayesian statistical modelling offers a powerful framework for modelling learning processes under conditions of uncertainty, allowing prediction models to update dynamically when new learner data become available.
However, despite these technological advances, there remains limited empirical research integrating blended learning environments, learning analytics, artificial intelligence, and Bayesian modelling within EFL academic writing instruction.
The present study seeks to address this gap.
3. Research Objectives
The study aims to achieve the following objectives:
To design an adaptive blended assessment framework that integrates face-to-face instruction with Moodle-based digital assessment.
To collect and analyse learning analytics data derived from students’ interactions with the Moodle learning management system.
To apply artificial intelligence techniques for analysing textual features and linguistic patterns in student essays.
To implement Bayesian statistical modelling in order to predict learner performance and update feedback mechanisms dynamically.
To examine the effectiveness of adaptive feedback in enhancing the academic writing competence of third-year EFL students.
4. Conceptual Framework
The conceptual framework of the study integrates four complementary theoretical domains.
4.1 Blended Learning
Blended learning refers to the systematic integration of face-to-face classroom instruction with online learning environments, allowing learners to benefit from both synchronous and asynchronous modes of instruction.
The concept was extensively theorized by Charles R. Graham, who defines blended learning as the deliberate combination of online and in-person instructional modalities in order to enhance learning effectiveness.
Source:
https://doi.org/10.4324/9780203876084
Within this study, blended learning provides the pedagogical infrastructure through which classroom writing instruction is complemented by Moodle-based digital activities.
4.2 Learning Analytics
Learning analytics involves the measurement, collection, analysis, and reporting of data about learners and their contexts in order to understand and optimise learning processes.
The field was formalised by scholars such as George Siemens and Dragan Gašević, who emphasize the role of educational data in supporting evidence-based pedagogical decisions.
Source:
https://doi.org/10.1145/2330601.2330605
In this study, learning analytics will be used to analyse behavioural indicators generated through the Moodle platform.
4.3 Artificial Intelligence in Education
Artificial Intelligence in Education (AIED) refers to the use of computational techniques to support learning, assessment, and instructional decision-making.
According to Wayne Holmes, AI systems can analyse learner interactions, identify patterns in student performance, and support automated formative feedback mechanisms.
Source:
https://doi.org/10.1787/9789264303012-en
Within this research, AI techniques will be applied to analyse linguistic features of student essays and detect writing patterns.
4.4 Bayesian Modelling in Learning Analytics
Bayesian modelling provides a probabilistic framework for representing learning processes and updating predictions as new evidence becomes available.
A comprehensive introduction to Bayesian machine learning is provided by Kevin P. Murphy, whose work emphasises the role of probabilistic inference in modelling complex systems.
Source:
https://probml.github.io/pml-book/
Bayesian approaches have also been applied in educational data mining research by scholars such as Ryan S. J. d. Baker.
Source:
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4614-3305-7
In this study, Bayesian modelling will be used to estimate the probability of student writing improvement based on behavioural and performance data.
5. Methodology
Research Design
The study adopts a case study design, focusing on the implementation of an adaptive blended assessment framework within a specific educational context.
Case study research allows an in-depth examination of complex educational phenomena within their real-world context.
Population and Context
Participants will consist of third-year EFL students enrolled at Belhadj Bouchaib University.
These students are expected to develop advanced academic writing skills, including argumentative and expository essay production.
6. Data Sources
The study will collect two main categories of data.
6.1 Writing Performance Data
Writing data will include:
pre-test essays
draft submissions
revised essays
final writing products
analytic rubric scores
teacher feedback records.
These data will enable the evaluation of students’ writing development over the course of the intervention.
6.2 Moodle Learning Analytics Data
Behavioural indicators extracted from the Moodle platform will include:
Variable	Description
Platform Logins	Number of times a student accesses Moodle
Resource Access	Number of learning materials viewed
Draft Submissions	Number of essay drafts uploaded
Revision Frequency	Number of revisions made
Feedback Access	Frequency of feedback consultation
Time on Task	Time spent on writing activities
These variables represent learner engagement and self-regulatory behaviour in the digital learning environment.
7. Artificial Intelligence Component
Artificial intelligence techniques will be employed to analyse textual and behavioural data generated by learners.
Possible approaches include:
Natural Language Processing (NLP) for analysing linguistic structures
Automated writing evaluation for identifying grammatical and lexical patterns
Machine learning classification models for detecting writing proficiency levels.
These techniques enable the automatic identification of writing difficulties and performance trends.
8. Bayesian Learning Analytics Model
The Bayesian model will estimate the probability of student performance based on behavioural evidence.
The fundamental probabilistic formulation is:
P(Performance | Behaviour Data)
Where behaviour data may include:
login frequency
revision behaviour
draft submission patterns
feedback engagement.
As new learner data become available, the model updates predictions using Bayesian posterior probability.
Advantages of this approach include:
modelling uncertainty in learning processes
dynamic updating of predictions
support for personalized feedback mechanisms.
9. Expected Pedagogical Contribution
The study aims to contribute to EFL didactics and educational technology research by proposing an integrated model of:
AI-supported adaptive blended assessment using Bayesian learning analytics.
Such a model may enable instructors to:
identify struggling writers at an early stage
provide personalized and data-driven feedback
support continuous improvement in academic writing performance
implement evidence-based teaching practices.
10. Keywords
Adaptive Assessment
Learning Analytics
Artificial Intelligence in Education
Bayesian Modelling
Academic Writing
Blended Learning
Moodle Analytics
EFL Pedagogy
✅ Important strategic advice for your thesis
Your research is methodologically powerful, but examiners will likely ask one critical question:
How exactly do learning analytics, AI, and Bayesian modelling interact in your adaptive feedback system?
Therefore, the next step for strengthening your dissertation is to present a clear operational model (an Adaptive Assessment Architecture Diagram) showing the full workflow:
Student behaviour → analytics → AI analysis → Bayesian prediction → adaptive feedback.

ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
Your table already captures the
core constructs linking writing product, learning processes, and learning analytics indicators
. To incorporate
help-seeking communication via Moodle private messages
as an additional predictor, it should be conceptualized as a
qualitative discourse-based learning analytics variable
reflecting
self-regulated help-seeking behaviour
.
In self-regulated learning theory,
help-seeking is considered an adaptive metacognitive strategy
where learners actively request clarification, guidance, or feedback to overcome learning difficulties (Zimmerman, 2008). Within LMS environments, these behaviours become
traceable interaction data
, allowing them to be operationalized in learning analytics research.
Below is a
refined extension of your framework
, integrating help-seeking communication as a measurable construct.

Operationalizing Help-Seeking Behaviour in Learning Analytics
In your methodology chapter, help-seeking communication can be operationalized through
three analytic dimensions
.
1. Frequency of Help-Seeking
Number of student-initiated messages requesting assistance.
Example indicators:
clarification requests about assignment requirements
requests for explanation of teacher feedback
questions about grammar or vocabulary
Interpretation:
Level
Interpretation
High
Active learner monitoring and engagement
Moderate

Low
Possible disengagement or lack of metacognitive awareness
2. Type of Help-Seeking
Messages can be coded using
qualitative discourse analysis
.
Possible categories:
Help-Seeking Type
Example
Clarification request
“I don't understand the comment about cohesion.”
Procedural question
“How many references should the essay include?”
Conceptual support
“Can you explain how to develop the argument?”
Language assistance
“Is this sentence grammatically correct?”
This coding allows
qualitative interpretation of learner needs
.
3. Feedback Uptake Through Communication
Another indicator is whether students
respond to feedback through dialogue
.
Example:
Teacher feedback → student message asking clarification → revised draft.
This interaction sequence reflects
active feedback engagement
, which is strongly linked to writing improvement.
Why Help-Seeking Is Important for Your Thesis
Including this variable strengthens your study in several ways.
1. It Captures Invisible Learning Processes
Learning analytics often focus on
clickstream behaviour
, but help-seeking captures
cognitive struggle and learner agency
.
2. It Reflects Self-Regulated Learning
Help-seeking is considered a
metacognitive regulation strategy
.
Zimmerman (2008) identifies adaptive help-seeking as a key component of
self-regulated learning cycles
.
3. It Enriches the Adaptive Feedback System
Students who actively request clarification can receive
targeted scaffolding
rather than generic feedback.

II. Product as a Multi-Dimensional Construct (Analytics-Based)
1-Analytic Rubric Layer (Pedagogical Product Quality)
Product Dimension	Moodle Data Source	Operational Measure	Feedback Sensitivity
Argument Development	Moodle Advanced Grading (Rubric criterion score)	Numerical score per submission	Triggers higher-order, reasoning-focused feedback
Coherence &amp; Cohesion	Moodle Advanced Grading (Rubric criterion score)	Numerical score per submission	Triggers structural and discourse-level feedback
Language Accuracy	Moodle Advanced Grading (Rubric criterion score)	Numerical score per submission	Triggers corrective linguistic feedback
Lexical Range	Moodle Advanced Grading (Rubric criterion score)	Numerical score per submission	Triggers lexical enrichment feedback
This layer ensures criterion-level adaptation, not generic summative comments.
2- Textual Analytics Layer (Computational Product Indicators) :
Indicator	Measurement Procedure	Sensitivity to Intervention
Error Density	Number of grammatical/mechanical errors per 100 words (automated or coded)	Detects response to corrective feedback
Lexical Diversity	Type-Token Ratio (unique words ÷ total words)	Detects vocabulary expansion after enrichment feedback
Syntactic Complexity	Mean clause length or clauses per sentence	Detects structural development following modelling
Cohesion Index	Frequency of logical connectors per 100 words	Detects discourse-level improvement
Text Length	Word count per draft	Detects elaboration and idea expansion
This layer ensures product enhancement is empirically measurable, not impressionistic.
3- Product Growth Layer :
Growth Indicator	Measurement Formula	Interpretation for Feedback Validation
Rubric Score	Final rubric − Draft 1 rubric	Measures pedagogical quality improvement
Error Density	Draft 1 errors − Final errors	Measures effectiveness of corrective feedback
Word Count	Final word count − Draft 1 word count	Measures elaboration growth
Cohesion Index	Final cohesion − Draft 1 cohesion	Measures discourse improvement
Lexical Diversity	Final TTR − Draft 1 TTR	Measures lexical development
Argument Depth	Structured reasoning coding (Draft comparison)	Measures higher-order cognitive development
This layer operationalises feedback effectiveness:
1-No measurable improvement  if   feedback not internalised.
2-Improvement aligned with feedback type if adaptive mechanism validated.
3-Selective improvement if partial feedback uptake.
This table is structured and analytically robust, suitable for direct inclusion in your methodology chapter under Product Operationalisation through Learning Analytics.
Feedback Type	Degree of Explicitness	Focus (Form / Process)	Cognitive Demand	When to Give It (Based on Analytics Indicators)	Theoretical Anchor
Direct Corrective Feedback	High	Form (grammar, syntax, lexis)	Low	- First draft shows repeated fossilized errors - High error density across categories - Minimal revision attempts	Dana Ferris; Formative assessment (Paul Black)
Indirect Corrective Feedback	Moderate	Form	Moderate	- Student attempts revision but errors persist - Moderate engagement in draft cycles - Partial linguistic control evident	Ferris (L2 corrective feedback research)
Metalinguistic Feedback	Moderate–High	Form + Rule Awareness	Moderate	- Recurrent patterned errors - Evidence of partial conceptual understanding - Suitable for intermediate-level writers	Sociocultural mediation (Lev Vygotsky)
Strategic (Higher-Order) Feedback	Moderate	Process (organization, thesis clarity, coherence)	High	- Surface errors reduced - Structural weaknesses visible - Drafts show engagement but weak argument development	Cognitive writing model (Linda Flower; John R. Hayes)
Metacognitive Feedback	Low explicitness	Process + Self-regulation	High	- Analytics show frequent revisions - Improved linguistic accuracy - Student ready for autonomous refinement	Self-regulated learning (Barry Zimmerman)
Dialogic Onsite Feedback	Adaptive (varies)	Integrated (Form + Process)	Adaptive	- After online revision cycle - When analytics reveal misunderstanding patterns - Before final submission to consolidate learning	Sociocultural scaffolding (Jerome Bruner)
Feedforward Guidance	Moderate	Process (future performance)	Moderate–High	- After final draft evaluation - When preparing for next writing task	Formative feedback model (John Hattie)
Affective-Motivational Feedback	Low	Engagement + Persistence	Low–Moderate	- Declining log activity - Delayed submissions - Low time-on-task patterns	Engagement theory in learning analytics (George Siemens)
Temporal Distribution Across the Writing Cycle
1-Early Draft Stage:Direct corrective feedback,Metalinguistic explanations;Limited strategic guidanc.Goal: Stabilize linguistic foundation within the learner’s ZPD. (Equipped smart phones so no need )
2-Mid-Revision Stage:Indirect corrective feedback,Strategic feedback on argumentation;Beginning metacognitive prompts .Goal: Shift from error correction to structural refinement.(Online)
2-Advanced Revision Stage:Metacognitive feedback / Dialogic onsite clarification/Feedforward planning. Goal: Foster autonomy and self-regulated revision. (Onsite)
Core Adaptive Principle in Your Thesis :Feedback is not linear.It is:Data-triggered.Graduated.Faded progressively  ;Learning analytics   indicators (revision frequency, error recurrence, activity logs, submission intervals) determine:When explicitness must increase;When cognitive demand must rise;When scaffolding must be withdrawn..This is how the model operationalizes the Zone of Proximal Development dynamically rather than theoretically.Cognitive demand in thesis refers to the level of mental processing required from the learner in order to act upon feedback.Grounded in self-regulated learning theory (Barry Zimmerman) and cognitive writing models (John R. Hayes), cognitive demand determines how much intellectual effort the learner must invest in revision. ;;; me ===It can even give u the amount of cognitive demand invested here
Briefly:Low cognitive demand → The learner receives the correction and applies it (e.g., direct correction).Moderate cognitive demand → The learner must identify and repair the error using cues (e.g., indirect feedback).High cognitive demand → The learner must evaluate, rethink, reorganize, or self-monitor (e.g., metacognitive prompts, strategic feedback).
In the adaptive model, cognitive demand increases progressively as analytics show growing learner competence.
Thus, cognitive demand is the regulated level of intellectual engagement required to transform feedback into learning.






New Evidence Observed:






Moderate lexical diversity

Using Bayes’ theorem (Pearl, 1988):
Probabilistic Reasoning in Intelligent Systems
The system updates probabilities:




Argument Structure probability of High increases from 0.30 → 0.55

This updating is automatic and dynamic.

System generates adaptive feedback:

Exactly. In other words, your adaptive system should go beyond simple grading and focus on underlying competencies that the student demonstrates through multiple observable behaviours.
Here’s what that means in practice:
1️⃣ Patterns of Evidence
Instead of just looking at the final essay score, your system considers:

Process indicators: e.g., number of revisions, pauses, or draft iterations


Behavioural indicators: e.g., time spent planning, frequency of consulting feedback


Product indicators: e.g., cohesion, lexical diversity, argument completeness


Metacognitive indicators: e.g., self-assessment reflections, goal-setting, feedback uptake

Each type of evidence provides partial information about latent writing skills.
2️⃣ Inferring Latent Competence
Using a probabilistic model like a Bayesian Network:

The system updates its estimate of each latent skill (e.g., argument structure, self-regulation) whenever new evidence is observed.


It does not assume a single score represents ability but treats observed behaviours as signals of underlying development.

3️⃣ Benefits

Dynamic adaptation: feedback adjusts to what the student actually demonstrates.


Theoretical alignment: connects observed behaviours to cognitive and metacognitive constructs.


Formative insight: teachers and learners see why a skill is developing or struggling, not just the final grade.
In short:
The system is not just “grading” essays; it is diagnosing the student’s writing development and guiding growth based on patterns, not points.

Adaptive Analytic Rubric for Academic Essay Writing (EFL Context)
Target population: Third-Year University EFL Students
Scale: 5-point analytic scale per criterion (1 = Very Limited → 5 = Advanced)
Total score: 35 points (7 criteria × 5)
1. Task Achievement &amp; Argumentative Positioning (5 points)
Construct definition: Extent to which the writer addresses the prompt, formulates a clear thesis, and sustains an argumentative stance.
Score	Descriptor
5	Explicit, precise thesis; fully addresses task; sustained and logically consistent argument.
4	Clear thesis; minor lapses in focus; task adequately addressed.
3	Thesis identifiable but partially developed; uneven task fulfillment.
2	Thesis unclear or weak; limited alignment with prompt.
1	No clear thesis; fails to address task.
Theoretical justification: Argumentative stance and disciplinary positioning are central to academic literacy development (Ken Hyland, 2005; 2019). Construct validity in writing assessment requires alignment with communicative purpose (Sara Cushing Weigle, 2002).
2. Organization &amp; Coherence (5 points)
Construct definition: Logical sequencing of ideas, paragraph unity, and effective use of cohesive devices.
Score	Descriptor
5	Logical progression; clear paragraph structure; cohesive devices used effectively and appropriately.
4	Generally coherent; minor issues in transitions.
3	Basic organization; mechanical or repetitive connectors.
2	Weak progression; limited cohesion.
1	Disorganized; ideas lack logical flow.
Theoretical justification: Coherence emerges from cohesive resources and thematic progression (M.A.K. Halliday &amp; Hasan, 1976). Academic writing competence requires control over discourse organization (Hyland, 2019).
3. Development of Ideas &amp; Critical Elaboration (5 points)
Construct definition: Depth of reasoning, explanation, exemplification, and analytical expansion of claims.
Score	Descriptor
5	Arguments critically elaborated; claims supported with relevant explanation or examples.
4	Adequate development; minor elaboration gaps.
3	Some support provided but underdeveloped.
2	Minimal explanation; assertions dominate.
1	Ideas largely undeveloped.
Theoretical justification: Academic writing requires epistemic elaboration and justification of claims (Hyland, 2019). Depth of support is a core dimension of analytic scoring (Weigle, 2002).
4. Lexical Resource &amp; Academic Vocabulary (5 points)
Construct definition: Range, precision, appropriateness, and sophistication of vocabulary in academic register.
Score	Descriptor
5	Wide and precise academic vocabulary; minimal lexical errors.
4	Good lexical range; occasional imprecision.
3	Adequate but repetitive vocabulary.
2	Limited range; frequent inappropriate word choice.
1	Very basic vocabulary; meaning often restricted.
Theoretical justification: Lexical sophistication correlates with advanced L2 writing proficiency and academic discourse control (Weigle, 2002; Hyland, 2005).
5. Grammatical Accuracy &amp; Syntactic Complexity (5 points)
Construct definition: Accuracy of grammatical structures and degree of syntactic variation.
Score	Descriptor
5	Consistently accurate; varied complex structures.
4	Generally accurate; some complex constructions.
3	Noticeable errors; limited complexity.
2	Frequent grammatical problems; simple structures dominate.
1	Persistent grammatical breakdown affecting meaning.
Theoretical justification: Analytic assessment enhances diagnostic validity and reliability in L2 writing evaluation (Weigle, 2002). Syntactic complexity reflects developmental progression in interlanguage (Ortega, 2003).
6. Academic Style, Register &amp; Stance (5 points)
Construct definition: Formal tone, appropriate hedging, absence of colloquial features, and control of authorial stance.
Score	Descriptor
5	Consistent academic register; effective hedging and stance control.
4	Mostly formal; minor stylistic inconsistencies.
3	Mixed register; limited hedging.
2	Frequent informal expressions.
1	Inappropriate conversational tone.
Theoretical justification: Academic stance and metadiscourse are central to disciplinary writing competence (Hyland, 2005).
7. Mechanics (5 points)
Construct definition: Spelling, punctuation, capitalization, formatting conventions.
Score	Descriptor
5	Virtually error-free mechanics.
4	Minor errors not affecting readability.
3	Noticeable mechanical errors.
2	Frequent mechanical issues.
1	Severe mechanical problems affecting readability.
Theoretical justification: Surface accuracy contributes to overall comprehensibility and scoring reliability in analytic frameworks (Weigle, 2002).

Adaptive Feedback Decision Tree for the Writing Analytics System

Step 4 — Adaptive Feedback Decision Rules
Rule 1 — Linguistic Difficulty
IF
grammar accuracy ≤ 2
AND error density high
THEN
Provide
direct corrective feedback
grammar-focused exercises.
Theoretical basis:
Ferris (2011) corrective feedback research.
Rule 2 — Structural Writing Weakness
IF
organization ≤ 2
AND cohesion index low
THEN
Provide
strategic feedback
focused on paragraph structure and argument flow.
Theoretical basis:
Flower &amp; Hayes (1981) cognitive writing model.
Rule 3 — Active Revision but Weak Argument
IF
revision frequency high
AND argument score ≤ 3
THEN
Provide
higher-order feedback
on claim–evidence structure.
Theoretical basis:
Hyland (2019) academic writing pedagogy.
Rule 4 — Feedback Ignored
IF
feedback viewed = FALSE
AND no revision occurs
THEN
Trigger
metacognitive prompt
:
“What specific strategy will you apply to address the feedback?”
Theoretical basis:
Zimmerman (2000) self-regulated learning.
Rule 5 — Help-Seeking Communication Detected
IF
student sends clarification message
AND revision activity follows
THEN
Provide
dialogic scaffolding feedback
.
Example:
“Your revision shows improvement in cohesion. Now focus on strengthening your counter-argument.”
Theoretical basis:
Vygotsky sociocultural scaffolding.
Step 5 — Adaptive Feedback Output
Feedback is
graduated according to cognitive demand
.
Feedback Type
Cognitive Demand
Purpose
Direct corrective
Low
stabilize linguistic accuracy
Indirect feedback
Moderate
promote error diagnosis
Strategic feedback
High
improve argument and organization
Metacognitive prompts
Very high
foster self-regulated writing
This progression reflects
adaptive scaffolding within the Zone of Proximal Development
.

After feedback is delivered:

.

Vous avez dit&nbsp;:
now in a short paragraph in simple words summarise this and how learning anlytics help improve learner acdemic writing
ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
In this study,
learning analytics helps improve students’ academic writing by analysing both how students write and what they write
. First, behavioural data from Moodle (such as time spent on tasks, number of drafts, revisions, feedback views, and help-seeking messages) are collected to understand students’ writing process and engagement. Second, the written texts themselves are analysed using an analytic rubric and simple text indicators (such as lexical diversity, cohesion markers, and grammatical errors) to evaluate writing quality. These process and product data are then analysed using statistical methods and machine learning models (such as clustering to identify learner profiles, Random Forest to predict writing performance, and Bayesian modelling to infer underlying writing competencies). Based on these analyses, the system generates
adaptive feedback tailored to each student’s needs
, helping learners revise their drafts, develop stronger arguments, improve coherence, and regulate their writing strategies over time. Thus, learning analytics transforms raw learning data into
targeted pedagogical support that gradually enhances students’ academic writing development
.

Student S01 initially demonstrated moderate behavioural engagement but limited paragraph development and weak discourse cohesion. ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
Exactly how it works is this:
learning analytics first collects observable evidence of writing behaviour and writing quality, then AI models transform that evidence into pedagogical decisions
. In your study, Moodle gives you behavioural data such as assignment views, rubric consultation, time-on-task, draft frequency, revision timing, feedback views, and help-seeking messages. The essays give you product data such as rubric scores, lexical diversity, cohesion markers, error density, argument depth, and word count. These two streams are combined so that you do not judge students only by the final paragraph, but by the
pattern of development
they show while composing, revising, and reacting to feedback. That is why writing development can be analysed through learning analytics: the system observes not only the final text, but also the learner’s process, regulation, and response to instruction.
Here is the exact logic of your system. A student writes a paragraph in Moodle. You extract Moodle behaviour indicators and analyse the paragraph with a rubric plus text features. Then you ask three different analytical questions.
Clustering
answers:
What type of learner is this student?
Random Forest
answers:
Which features predict writing quality or risk?
Bayesian modelling
answers:
Given all this evidence, what is the probability that the student has low, medium, or high competence in argumentation, cohesion, self-regulation, or linguistic control?
After that, you give adaptive feedback onsite, the student revises, and you measure whether the revision improved in the exact area targeted by the feedback. That is how analytics-informed feedback supports writing improvement.
1. What AI should do in your study
You do not need a huge AI system. You need a
small research tool
that helps you with three jobs:
A. Text analytics
The AI tool should automatically extract writing indicators from each draft, such as:
word count
sentence count
average sentence length
lexical diversity
cohesion markers
grammatical error count
academic vocabulary frequency
This supports product analysis.
B. Data integration
The tool should combine:
Moodle behavioural indicators
rubric scores
text analytics indicators
revision indicators
feedback engagement indicators
into one dataset where
each row = one student-task-draft observation
.
C. Prediction support
The tool should prepare data for:
clustering
Random Forest
Bayesian inference
So the AI tool is not replacing your role as teacher-researcher. It is helping you
extract indicators, organise data, and support interpretation
.
2. What to program exactly
You should program a simple pipeline with four modules.
Module 1: Import data
Input:
Moodle CSV logs
rubric scores
essay texts
message/help-seeking records
Output:
cleaned dataframe
Module 2: Extract text features
For each essay, calculate:
word_count
type_token_ratio
connector_count
avg_sentence_length
error_density
academic_vocab_count
Module 3: Merge all features
Create one table like this:
student_id

rubric_argument
rubric_cohesion
ttr
error_density
revisions
feedback_views
help_seeking
Module 4: Run models
clustering for learner profiles
Random Forest for prediction
Bayesian model for latent competence inference

4. What the AI tool is for, exactly
Your AI tool should help answer four research questions:
Question 1
How do students behave while writing in Moodle?
Use:
descriptive statistics
correlations
sequence/process indicators
Question 2
What learner profiles exist?
Use:
clustering
Question 3
Which factors predict writing quality or writing improvement?
Use:
Random Forest
Question 4
What latent competencies are likely developing underneath the visible behaviour?
Use:
Bayesian modelling
So the AI tool is for
feature extraction, prediction, profiling, and competence inference
.
5. When and how to use clustering
Use clustering
after you have cleaned the dataset and standardised the main behavioural/process variables
.
Its purpose is not prediction. Its purpose is
profiling
.
For example, use these variables:
time_on_task
revision_frequency
feedback_views
resource_access
help_seeking_frequency
draft_count
Then apply K-means or hierarchical clustering.
What it gives you:
Cluster 1: highly engaged strategic revisers
Cluster 2: superficially engaged late submitters
Cluster 3: low-engagement at-risk learners
How to use it in your thesis:
to classify learners into patterns
to compare whether clusters differ in writing outcomes
to justify differentiated feedback pathways
In other words, clustering tells you
who your learners are as process profiles
.
6. When and how to use Random Forest
Use Random Forest
after feature extraction
, when you want to predict an outcome.
You can use it in two ways.
A. Random Forest Regressor
Use it to predict:
final rubric score
improvement score
argument development score
Target variable example:
final_total_score
or
score_gain = final_score - draft1_score
Predictors can include:
time_on_task
revision_frequency
feedback_views
help_seeking
ttr
cohesion_index
error_density
draft_count
What it gives you:
predicted score
feature importance
This answers:
Which variables matter most for writing improvement?
B. Random Forest Classifier
Use it to classify:
at-risk vs not at-risk
low / medium / high improvement
low / medium / high writer profile
Example target:
risk_status
What it gives you:
predicted category
feature importance
Use Random Forest when your question is:
Can I predict performance or risk from process and product indicators?
7. When and how to use Bayesian modelling
Use Bayesian modelling
after you have theoretical constructs you want to infer but cannot directly observe
.
In your case, latent competencies may include:
argument structure competence
cohesion competence
linguistic complexity
self-regulated revision
These are not directly visible. What you observe are indicators such as:
rubric argument score
number of global revisions
lexical diversity
feedback uptake
help-seeking frequency
Bayesian modelling is useful because it lets you say:
Given the observed evidence, the student has a 0.68 probability of medium-to-high self-regulated revision competence.
That is much more theoretically meaningful than a raw score alone.
Use Bayesian modelling when your question is:
What underlying writing competence is most likely present, given the observable evidence?
So:
Random Forest = prediction
clustering = grouping/profiling
Bayesian = inference of latent competence
8. Are these the only methods needed?
No, and they should not stand alone. Your study also needs the foundational analyses underneath them:
descriptive statistics
paired t-test or Wilcoxon for pre/post writing improvement
correlation analysis
rubric analysis
textual feature analysis
Those are essential. The advanced models sit
on top of
those basics.
A sound structure is:
descriptive analysis of Moodle behaviour
rubric and text-feature analysis of writing quality
clustering for learner profiles
Random Forest for prediction
Bayesian modelling for latent competence inference
interpretation of how feedback matched learner needs and revision outcomes
That is enough. You do not need many more algorithms unless you want a comparison model for methodological strength.
9. If you want one comparison model
You may optionally add
one simpler benchmark model
, such as:
linear regression for score prediction
or
logistic regression for risk classification
Why? Because then you can show that Random Forest performs better than a simpler traditional model. That strengthens your methodology.
But if your dataset is not large, do not overload the thesis with too many models.

Here is the
clear division of roles between the AI system and you as the researcher/teacher
, stated briefly.
Method
AI Role
Your Role (Researcher/Instructor)
Descriptive statistics
Calculate averages, distributions, and visualizations of behavioural data
Interpret what the patterns mean for student engagement and writing behaviour
Rubric analysis
Store and organise rubric scores in the dataset
Evaluate essays and assign rubric scores based on academic writing criteria
Text analytics
Automatically extract text indicators (word count, lexical diversity, cohesion markers, error density)
Interpret what these indicators reveal about writing development
Clustering
Detect learner groups based on behavioural and writing indicators
Interpret the meaning of each learner profile and connect it to pedagogy
Random Forest
Predict writing performance or risk and identify the most influential variables
Interpret predictions and use them to guide instructional decisions
Bayesian modelling
Estimate probabilities of underlying writing competencies based on evidence
Interpret competence levels and diagnose students’ strengths and weaknesses
Adaptive feedback analysis
Organise analytics results and suggest possible feedback triggers
Deliver the actual feedback, guide revision, and support student learning
In simple terms:
AI analyses data and detects patterns.
You interpret the results and transform them into teaching actions and feedback.
So the AI system performs
data processing and prediction
, while you perform
pedagogical interpretation and adaptive instruction
.

as

.

.

from
pgmpy
.
inference
import
VariableElimination
df
=
pd
.
read_csv(
"outputs/dataset_with_clusters.csv"
)
# Convert continuous variables to simple categories
df
[
"rev_level"
]

.
cut(

[

],

=

,

,

,

],

,
"medium"
,
"high"
])

=

.
cut(

],

[

,

,

,
5
],

"low"
,
"medium"
,
"high"
])

=

.
cut(

=

-
1
,

,
2
,

],

,
"medium"
,
"high"

9. What each script does
clean_data.py
→ builds dataset
text_features.py
→ extracts writing indicators
clustering.py
→ finds learner profiles
random_forest.py
→ predicts score/risk
bayesian_model.py
→ estimates latent competence
That is your whole AI pipeline.
10. What you do, step by step
As researcher, your order is:
Step 1
Export Moodle logs and rubric scores.
Step 2
Put essays into CSV with columns like:
student_id
task_id
draft_no
essay_text
Step 3
Run:
Bash
python scripts/clean_data.py
python scripts/text_features.py
python scripts/clustering.py
python scripts/random_forest.py
python scripts/bayesian_model.py
Step 4
Read outputs and interpret them pedagogically.
For example:
cluster 2 = disengaged students
Random Forest says revision frequency is strongest predictor
Bayesian model says student has medium cohesion competence
Then you use that to give adaptive feedback.
11. What not to do at the beginning
Do not start with:
a website
a chatbot
a mobile app
deep learning
a complex dashboard
Start with
CSV files + Python scripts
. That is enough for a PhD study.
12. Your minimum viable system
Your first working version only needs:
one dataset
one text feature script
one clustering model
one Random Forest model
one simple Bayesian model
That is already a real AI-assisted research system.
13. Best starting point for you
Your first practical goal should be this:
Build one small dataset with 5 students and 1 writing task, then run text features + Random Forest.
Do not begin with the whole thesis dataset.
Begin with a pilot.
14. In very simple words
You are not “programming artificial intelligence” in the science-fiction sense.
You are building a tool that:
reads student data
measures writing features
groups learners
predicts outcomes
helps you decide what feedback to give
That is exactly the kind of AI your study needs.

5.
scripts/text_features.py
Python
Exécuter
import
pandas
as
pd
import
re
CONNECTORS
=
[
"however"
,
"therefore"
,
"furthermore"
,
"moreover"
,
"in addition"
,
"because"
,
"as a result"
,
"for example"
]
def
tokenize
(
text
:
str
):
return
re
.
findall(
r"\b\w+\b"
,
str
(
text
)
.
lower())
def
word_count
(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
int
:
return
len
(
tokenize
(
text
))
def
type_token_ratio
(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
float
:
words
=
tokenize
(
text
)
if
not
words
:
return
0.0
return
len
(
set
(
words
))
/
len
(
words
)
def
cohesion_index
(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
int
:
lowered
=
str
(
text
)
.
lower()
return
sum
(
lowered
.
count(
connector
)
for
connector
in
CONNECTORS
)
def
sentence_count
(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
int
:
sentences
=
re
.
split(
r"[.!?]+"
,
str
(
text
))
sentences
=
[
s
.
strip()
for
s
in
sentences
if
s
.
strip()]
return
len
(
sentences
)
def
avg_sentence_length
(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
float
:
wc
=
word_count
(
text
)
sc
=
sentence_count
(
text
)
if
sc
==
0
:
return
0.0
return
wc
/
sc
df
=
pd
.
read_csv(
"outputs/merged_dataset.csv"
)
df
[
"word_count"
]
=
df
[
"essay_text"
]
.
apply(
word_count
)
df
[
"ttr"
]
=
df
[
"essay_text"
]
.
apply(

)
df
[
"cohesion_index"
]
=
df
[
"essay_text"
]
.
apply(
cohesion_index
)
df
[
"sentence_count"
]
=
df
[
"essay_text"
]
.
apply(

=

10. What each script gives you
clean_data.py
merges Moodle, rubric, and essay data
text_features.py
adds writing indicators such as word count, TTR, and cohesion
clustering.py
groups learners into profiles
random_forest.py
predicts scores and shows the most important predictors
bayesian_model.py
estimates probable competence level from evidence
11. What you do with the results
You interpret them like this:
if
cluster
shows low engagement, the student may need motivational or planning support
if
Random Forest
shows revision frequency is highly important, revision becomes a major pedagogical focus
if
Bayesian inference
suggests low argument competence, you give higher-order feedback on claim, evidence, and reasoning

, the AI tool in your study is not only a technical program. It must contain
structured educational components
that allow the system to interpret learning data and connect it to writing instruction. In other words, the AI needs
pedagogical knowledge inputs
that you, as the researcher and instructor, design in advance.
Below is
what you should prepare and “fill” inside the AI tool
so that it can support adaptive academic writing assessment.

The rule-based interpretation layer translated behavioural, textual, and rubric-based indicators into pedagogically meaningful diagnoses. 3. Threshold Definitions
This is the key methodological point:
thresholds should be defensible, transparent, and adjustable
. In your thesis, do not present them as universal standards. Present them as
task-sensitive operational cut-points

ETS

Recommended wording for the thesis
Thresholds were defined as operational decision rules to identify cases requiring pedagogical intervention. Because no single universal cut-score exists for behavioural and textual indicators in L2 writing analytics, thresholds were set provisionally on the basis of task expectations, rubric scale interpretation, and pilot observation, and were intended to be refined after initial implementation.

4. How to justify the thresholds academically
You need to justify them at three levels.
A. Task-based justification
A threshold must fit the writing task. For example, a body paragraph assigned as an argumentative support paragraph cannot reasonably be evaluated with the same word-count expectation as a full essay. That is why
word-count thresholds must be tied to task design
, not imported blindly.
B. Rubric-based justification
For 1–5 analytic scales, scores of
1–2 are usually interpreted as clear areas of weakness
,
3 as developing/moderate
, and
4–5 as strong performance
. This is consistent with the logic of analytic diagnosis in writing assessment.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
C. Pilot-based justification
For behavioural indicators like time-on-task, revision frequency, and feedback delay, the strongest justification is empirical: you calibrate them from your own pilot data. For example, you may later redefine “low time-on-task” as the
bottom quartile
of your sample rather than a fixed number. That makes the model more valid and more context-sensitive. This is also more consistent with learning analytics practice, which is context-dependent rather than universal.
SAGE Journals
+1
5. A more defensible version: threshold bands
Instead of only one cut-point, use
bands
. This is often better for doctoral work.

1–2
3

This lets you say the system does not make crude binary judgments; it uses
graduated pedagogical sensitivity
.
6. Full methodological justification paragraph you can use
The rule-based interpretation layer functioned as the pedagogical reasoning component of the adaptive assessment system. Drawing on Evidence-Centered Design, the system linked observable indicators from Moodle logs, rubric scores, and textual analytics to instructional claims about learner needs. For example, low organization scores combined with weak cohesion indicators were interpreted as discourse-level writing difficulties, while limited revision activity was treated as evidence of weak self-regulated monitoring. Thresholds were defined as provisional task-sensitive cut-points rather than universal standards, because the pedagogical meaning of behavioural and textual indicators depends on the writing task, rubric design, and local learner context. Accordingly, thresholds were initially set using task expectations and analytic scale logic, and were intended to be refined through pilot calibration and distributional analysis of the study sample. This structure enabled the system to translate raw learner data into targeted adaptive feedback decisions.
srhe.tandfonline.com

"supporting_evidence"
-
"explanation"
-
"transition"
learning_goal:
-
"develop_argument"
-
"use_cohesion"
-
"use_academic_vocabulary"
B. Analytic rubric file
This gives the AI the assessment logic.
File:
config/rubric.yaml
YAML
rubric:
scale: [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]
criteria:
- name:
"task_achievement"
description:
"Addresses prompt and sustains argumentative position"
- name:
"organization"
description:
"Logical sequencing and paragraph structure"
- name:
"argumentation"
description:
"Claim, evidence, explanation, and reasoning"
- name:
"cohesion"

"Use of transitions and discourse links"

"lexical_resource"

"Range and appropriateness of vocabulary"

"grammar_accuracy"

"Formal register and stance"

C. Rule-based interpretation file
This is the pedagogical brain of the system.
File:
config/rules.yaml
YAML
rules:
- rule_id: R1
if:
organization_score:
"&lt;=2"
cohesion_index:
"&lt;2"
interpretation:
"discourse_organization_problem"
feedback_type:
"strategic_feedback"
intervention:
"paragraph_restructuring_support"
justification:
"Low organization plus weak cohesion indicates discourse-level writing difficulty."
- rule_id: R2
if:
argumentation_score:
"&lt;=2"
interpretation:
"weak_argument_development"
feedback_type:
"higher_order_feedback"
intervention:
"claim_evidence_reasoning_scaffold"
justification:
"Low argument score signals underdeveloped reasoning."
- rule_id: R3
if:
grammar_accuracy_score:
"&lt;=2"
error_density:
"&gt;8"
interpretation:
"linguistic_accuracy_problem"
feedback_type:
"direct_corrective_feedback"
intervention:
"grammar_exercises"
justification:
"Persistent high error density plus low grammar score signals form-level difficulty."
- rule_id: R4
if:
revision_frequency:
"&lt;1"
interpretation:
"weak_self_regulated_revision"
feedback_type:
"metacognitive_prompt"
intervention:
"guided_revision_checklist"
justification:
"Minimal revision indicates weak monitoring and control."
- rule_id: R5
if:
rubric_views:
"&lt;1"
interpretation:
"low_assessment_literacy"
feedback_type:
"criteria_referenced_feedback"
intervention:
"rubric_walkthrough"
justification:
"Minimal rubric consultation indicates weak criteria awareness."
- rule_id: R6
if:
feedback_views:
"&gt;=1"
revision_frequency:
"&lt;1"
interpretation:
"low_feedback_uptake"
feedback_type:
"feedforward_feedback"
intervention:
"feedback_decoding_session"
justification:
"Viewing feedback without revising suggests weak feedback literacy."
- rule_id: R7
if:
help_seeking_messages:
"&gt;=1"
interpretation:
"adaptive_help_seeking"
feedback_type:
"dialogic_scaffolding"
intervention:
"onsite_clarification"
justification:
"Help-seeking reflects emerging self-regulated learning."

low:
"&lt;80"

"80-119"
strong:
"&gt;=120"

low_risk:
"&lt;4"
watch:
"4-8"

"&gt;8"

low:
"0"
moderate:
"1"
strong:
"&gt;=2"

"&lt;15"

"15-29"

"&gt;=30"

E. Feedback template file
This tells the AI what feedback to output when a rule is triggered.
File:
config/feedback_templates.yaml
YAML
feedback_templates:
strategic_feedback:
template:
&gt;
Your paragraph has a relevant idea, but the organization and cohesion need improvement.
Add a clearer topic sentence, develop one supporting point, and use transitions such as
"furthermore", "in addition", or "as a result".
direct_corrective_feedback:
template:
&gt;
Your paragraph contains repeated grammatical problems. Focus on verb tense, article use,
and sentence structure. Revise the highlighted sentences and complete the short grammar practice task.
higher_order_feedback:
template:
&gt;
Your paragraph needs stronger argumentation. Add one concrete example and explain how it supports your claim.
metacognitive_prompt:
template:
&gt;
Before revising, ask yourself: What is the main weakness in this paragraph? What one change will improve it most?
criteria_referenced_feedback:
template:
&gt;
Re-read the rubric before revising. Pay particular attention to organization, argumentation, and cohesion criteria.
feedforward_feedback:
template:
&gt;
You viewed the feedback, but the draft does not yet show substantial revision. Use the comments to make at least
one content-level and one cohesion-level change.
dialogic_scaffolding:
template:
&gt;
Your message shows active engagement. During the onsite session, we will clarify the feedback and model one revision together.

3. What your AI should calculate automatically
These are the features the system should generate from student texts and logs.
Text features
word_count
sentence_count
avg_sentence_length
lexical_diversity
or
ttr
cohesion_index
error_density
academic_vocab_count
Behaviour features
assignment_views
rubric_views
time_on_task
revision_frequency
feedback_views
help_seeking_messages
resource_access_count
first_access_delay_minutes
Derived growth features
score_gain = draft2_total - draft1_total
word_growth = draft2_word_count - draft1_word_count
cohesion_gain = draft2_cohesion_index - draft1_cohesion_index
error_reduction = draft1_error_density - draft2_error_density
4. The exact architecture you should program
Your AI should run in this order:
Step 1: Merge all data
Use
pandas.read_csv()
and
DataFrame.merge()
to combine logs, rubric scores, essays, and help-seeking records into one dataframe.
pandas.pydata.org
+1
Step 2: Extract textual indicators
Use
spaCy
for tokenization and sentence segmentation; a simple rule-based sentencizer is available when you do not need a full parser.
spacy.io
+2
spacy.io
+2
Step 3: Apply threshold rules
Read
thresholds.yaml
and label each indicator as low / moderate / strong.
Step 4: Apply didactic rules
Read
rules.yaml
. If a condition is met, assign:
pedagogical interpretation
feedback type
onsite intervention
Step 5: Run clustering
Use
KMeans
on behavioural + textual variables to identify learner profiles; scikit-learn documents KMeans and recommends k-means++ initialization, with silhouette analysis as one way to choose cluster count.
scikit-learn.org
+1
Use variables like:
time_on_task
revision_frequency
feedback_views
help_seeking_messages
lexical_diversity
cohesion_index
Step 6: Run Random Forest
Use
RandomForestRegressor
to predict
total_score
or
score_gain
, and
RandomForestClassifier
if you want to classify learners as at-risk / not at-risk. Scikit-learn provides both estimators.
scikit-learn.org
+1
Use predictors like:
assignment_views
rubric_views
time_on_task
revision_frequency
feedback_views
word_count
ttr
cohesion_index
error_density
Step 7: Run Bayesian inference
Use a discrete Bayesian network in
pgmpy
with latent competence nodes and observable evidence nodes, then perform inference to estimate the probability of low / medium / high competence. Pgmpy explicitly supports discrete Bayesian networks and inference workflows.
pgmpy
+2
pgmpy
+2

You are an academic writing feedback assistant for EFL argumentative writing.
Task:
- Genre: argumentative body paragraph
- Expected word range: 120-150
Student diagnosis:
- Organization score: 2/5
- Argumentation score: 2/5
- Cohesion index: 1
- Revision frequency: 0
- Feedback views: 1
- Help-seeking messages: 1
Triggered pedagogical rules:
- discourse_organization_problem
- weak_self_regulated_revision
- adaptive_help_seeking

- concise
- supportive
- criteria-referenced
- actionable
Generate:
1. strengths
2. areas for improvement
3. one revision task

That is what you give the generative AI, not the full methodology.

# Adaptive Writing AI Assistant
This project supports an adaptive blended assessment study in EFL academic writing.
## What it does
- Merges Moodle logs, rubric scores, essays, and help-seeking records
- Extracts text analytics features
- Applies threshold bands
- Applies pedagogical interpretation rules
- Builds learner profiles with clustering
- Predicts writing scores using Random Forest
- Infers latent competence levels using Bayesian modelling
- Generates adaptive feedback and onsite intervention suggestions
## Setup
1. Install Python 3.10+
2. Install dependencies:
```bash
pip install -r requirements.txt
Run
From the project root:
Bash
python scripts/run_pipeline.py
Outputs
Generated files will appear in
outputs/
.
---
# 4. `config/tasks.yaml`

expected_time_minutes: [30, 40]
expected_structure:
- "topic_sentence"
- "supporting_evidence"
- "explanation"
- "transition"
learning_goals:
- "develop_argument"
- "use_cohesion"
- "use_academic_vocabulary"

8.
config/feedback_templates.yaml
YAML
feedback_templates:
strategic_feedback:
&gt;
Your paragraph has a relevant idea, but its organization and cohesion need improvement.
Strengthen the topic sentence, develop one supporting point clearly, and use linking expressions
such as "furthermore", "in addition", or "as a result".
higher_order_feedback:
&gt;
Your paragraph needs stronger argument development.
Add one clear example or piece of evidence, then explain how it supports your claim.
direct_corrective_feedback:
&gt;
Your paragraph shows repeated grammatical difficulties.
Focus on tense consistency, article use, and sentence structure.
Revise the highlighted sentences and correct the repeated pattern.
metacognitive_prompt:
&gt;
Before revising, identify the main weakness in your paragraph.
Decide which one change would improve it most, then revise with that goal in mind.
criteria_referenced_feedback:
&gt;
Re-read the rubric before revising.
Focus especially on organization, argumentation, and cohesion so that your paragraph matches the task criteria.
feedforward_feedback:
&gt;
You viewed the feedback, but your text does not yet show sufficient revision.
Make at least one content-level change and one cohesion-level change before resubmitting.
dialogic_scaffolding:
&gt;
Your message shows active engagement with the writing task.
During the onsite session, we will clarify the feedback and revise one sentence together.
elaboration_feedback:
&gt;
Your paragraph is shorter than expected for this task.
Add one supporting detail and one explanatory sentence to develop the argument more fully.

def

CONNECTORS
=

"however"
,
"therefore"
,
"furthermore"
,
"moreover"
,
"in addition"
,
"as a result"
,
"for example"
,
"because"

]
ACADEMIC_WORDS
=

"furthermore"
,
"therefore"
,
"moreover"
,
"consequently"
,
"significant"
,
"flexibility"
,
"effectively"

,
"argument"
,
"evidence"
,
"cohesion"
,
}
def

(

:
str
) -&gt;
int
:
return
len
(
tokenize
(
text
))
def

(

:

int
:

=

.
split(
r"[.!?]+"
,

(

([

for

in

if
p
.
strip()])
def

(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
float
:

=
tokenize
(
text
)
return
len
(
set
(
words
))
/
len
(
words
)
if
words
else
0.0
def

(

:

) -&gt;
int
:

=

(

)
.

(

.

)

in

)
def

(

:

) -&gt;
float
:

=

(

)

=

(

/

if

else
0.0
def

(

:

) -&gt;
int
:

=

(

(

))

(

in

in

)
def
error_density_proxy
(
text
:
str
) -&gt;
float
:
"""
Simple starter proxy.
Replace with a more advanced grammar checker later.
"""
text
=
str
(
text
)
words
=
tokenize
(
text
)
if
not
words
:
return
0.0
rough_errors
=
0
if
"is very useful for students"
in
text
.
lower():
rough_errors
+=
1
if
len
(
words
)
&lt;
60
:
rough_errors
+=

text
.
count(
"good"
)
&gt;
0
:
rough_errors
+=
1
if
text
.
count(
"very"
)
&gt;
1
:
rough_errors
+=
1
return

((
rough_errors
/

(

))
*

,

)

def

,

(

.

,

))

(

,

):
return

3. The exact pedagogical variables to encode
Planning / forethought
These come from SRL theory: weak planning, delayed engagement, and limited criteria consultation indicate weak forethought regulation. Zimmerman’s model explicitly frames learning in forethought, performance, and self-reflection phases.
link.springer.com
+1

assignment_views

first_access_delay_minutes

Drafting / text production
These reflect writing-process engagement and text development. Hyland’s work on L2 writing and Weigle’s analytic assessment framing justify treating organization, argument, cohesion, and lexical control as distinct pedagogical constructs.

+1

word_count
avg_sentence_length

cohesion_index

Revision / monitoring and control
Revision behaviour is central to writing development and SRL. Students who revise little or only superficially are not using performance-phase monitoring effectively.
Springer
+1

feedback_to_revision_delay

Reflection / feedback uptake
Carless and Boud’s feedback literacy model justifies interpreting “feedback viewed but not acted upon” as weak feedback uptake rather than successful self-regulation.

4. Thresholds the engineer should program
These are
provisional pedagogical cut-points
, not universal standards. They are justified by task expectations, rubric scale logic, and pilot calibration. Weigle supports analytic criterion-based diagnosis; Zimmerman supports phase-based behavioural interpretation; Carless and Boud support interpreting action on feedback, not merely exposure to feedback, as meaningful uptake.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+2
Springer
+2
Threshold set to implement
For a
body paragraph task
expecting about
120–150 words
:
word_count
low:
&lt; 80
moderate:
80–119
satisfactory:
120–150
extended:
&gt; 150
Justification:
under 80 words usually signals underdevelopment for a paragraph expected to include claim, support, and explanation.
time_on_task_minutes
low:
&lt; 15
moderate:
15–29
satisfactory:
30–40
high:
&gt; 40
Justification:
less than 15 minutes for a planned academic paragraph often indicates insufficient planning and development rather than efficient completion.
rubric_views
low:
0
moderate:
1
strong:
&gt;= 2
Justification:
zero consultation suggests weak criteria awareness; criteria awareness is a core part of assessment literacy.
revision_frequency
low:
0
moderate:
1
strong:
&gt;= 2
Justification:
zero revision indicates product submission without meaningful monitoring.
error_density
per 100 words
low risk:
&lt; 4
watch:
4–8
high risk:
&gt; 8
Justification:
this is a practical intervention threshold: above 8 per 100 words suggests form-level difficulty is recurrent enough to merit focused support.
cohesion_index
for a paragraph
low:
&lt; 2
moderate:
2–3
strong:
&gt;= 4
Justification:
an argumentative body paragraph normally needs at least some visible discourse connection, not isolated sentences.
criterion scores
on a 1–5 rubric
low:
1–2
moderate:
3
strong:
4–5
Justification:
this follows standard analytic rubric logic: 1–2 indicates clear need for support, 3 indicates developing control, 4–5 indicates relative strength.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
feedback uptake
weak uptake: feedback viewed
&gt;= 1
and no revision in next cycle
active uptake: feedback viewed and at least one measurable revision
Justification:
feedback literacy requires using feedback, not merely opening it.
ERIC
+1
help-seeking
absent:
0
present:
&gt;= 1
Justification:
learner-initiated clarification is evidence of adaptive regulation when linked to revision action.
5. Interpretation rules the engineer should program
These rules are the didactic core of the system. Each rule must map
condition → pedagogical interpretation → feedback type → onsite intervention
.
Rule block A: planning and criteria awareness
Rule A1
If
rubric_views = 0
and
first_access_delay_minutes
is high, interpret as:
weak forethought regulation and low criteria awareness
Feedback:
criteria-referenced prompt
planning scaffold
Onsite intervention:
rubric walkthrough
model outline for the paragraph
Theoretical basis:
Zimmerman’s forethought phase and assessment literacy logic.
Springer
+1
Rule A2
If
time_on_task &lt; 15
and
word_count &lt; 80
, interpret as:
insufficient planning and underdeveloped task engagement
Feedback:
elaboration prompt
staged drafting prompt
Onsite intervention:
guided planning and expansion support
Basis:
weak forethought and shallow text production.
Rule block B: product quality
Rule B1
If
organization_score &lt;= 2
and
cohesion_index &lt; 2
, interpret as:
discourse organization problem
Feedback:
strategic feedback on paragraph structure and transitions
Onsite intervention:
paragraph restructuring support using a model
Basis:
Hyland’s discourse-oriented writing pedagogy and Weigle’s analytic diagnosis.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+1
Rule B2
If
argumentation_score &lt;= 2
, interpret as:
weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure
Feedback:
higher-order feedback on development of support
Onsite intervention:
claim–evidence–explanation scaffold
Basis:
academic writing competence requires explicit reasoning and support.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+1
Rule B3
If
grammar_accuracy_score &lt;= 2
and
error_density &gt; 8
, interpret as:
persistent linguistic accuracy problem
Feedback:
direct corrective or metalinguistic feedback
Onsite intervention:
focused grammar mini-lesson on recurrent pattern
Basis:
form-focused support is appropriate when error recurrence is high and clearly constrains performance.
Rule B4
If
lexical_resource_score &lt;= 2
and
ttr
is low, interpret as:
restricted lexical repertoire
Feedback:
lexical enrichment feedback
replacement list for repetition
Onsite intervention:
vocabulary expansion with model academic alternatives
Rule block C: revision and feedback uptake
Rule C1
If
revision_frequency = 0
, interpret as:
weak self-regulated revision
Feedback:
metacognitive revision prompt
Onsite intervention:
guided revision checklist
Basis:
monitoring and control are central in SRL performance phase.
Springer
+1
Rule C2
If
feedback_views &gt;= 1
and
revision_frequency = 0
, interpret as:
low feedback uptake / weak feedback literacy
Feedback:
feedforward message explaining how to operationalize comments
Onsite intervention:
feedback decoding session
Basis:
students need to interpret and act on feedback, not merely receive it.
ERIC
+1
Rule C3
If
revision_frequency &gt;= 1
but changes are only surface-level and
argumentation_score
remains low, interpret as:
revision limited to editing, not meaning-level development
Feedback:
strategic feedback
Onsite intervention:
model global revision moves
Rule block D: help-seeking
Rule D1
If
help_seeking_messages &gt;= 1
and message type is
feedback_clarification
or
argument_help
, interpret as:
adaptive help-seeking
Feedback:
dialogic scaffolding
Onsite intervention:
short teacher-student clarification conference
Basis:
adaptive help-seeking is a form of self-regulation when learners recognize difficulty and seek targeted support. This follows SRL logic and feedback literacy work.
Springer
+1
Rule D2
If performance is weak and
help_seeking_messages = 0
, interpret as:
possible passive disengagement or unrecognized difficulty
Feedback:
motivational and metacognitive prompt
Onsite intervention:
proactive teacher check-in

7. What you should tell the engineer about theory
Use this wording:
“Implement thresholds and interpretation rules as
pedagogical decision rules
, not as universal psychometric cut-scores. Their rationale comes from:
(1)
analytic writing assessment
for criterion-based diagnosis of writing dimensions, especially Weigle;
(2)
second language writing pedagogy
for discourse, argument, and revision, especially Hyland;
(3)
self-regulated learning theory
for forethought, performance, and self-reflection, especially Zimmerman;
(4)
feedback literacy
for interpreting whether students use feedback, especially Carless and Boud.”
That theoretical integration is defensible because it links observable data to claims about writing ability, regulation, and feedback use.

Short answer:
No — the document I generated mainly specifies the didactic logic (thresholds, interpretation rules, feedback triggers).
It
does not yet explicitly specify where and how to use
:
Clustering
Random Forest
Bayesian modelling
Those three methods must be
clearly included in the engineer specification
, because they are the
analytical engines
that transform the data into predictions and competence inference in your study.
Below is the
exact content you should add to the engineer specification
so the AI tool fully reflects your methodological framework.
Section to Add in the Engineer File
Learning Analytics and AI Modelling Layer
The AI tool must implement
three complementary analytical mechanisms
that support adaptive writing assessment:
Learner profiling (Clustering)
Performance prediction (Random Forest)
Competence inference (Bayesian modelling)
These mechanisms operate
after the rule-based diagnostic layer
and before the final adaptive feedback generation.
1. Learner Profiling Using Clustering
Objective
Identify
behavioural engagement patterns among students
based on Moodle learning analytics and writing indicators.
Data Inputs
Process indicators:
time-on-task
revision frequency
feedback views
rubric consultation
assignment access frequency
help-seeking messages
Product indicators:
rubric total score
cohesion index
lexical diversity
error density
word count
Algorithm
Use
K-Means clustering
.
Why K-Means
K-Means is appropriate because:
it identifies patterns in multidimensional learner behaviour
it groups students with similar engagement and writing profiles
it is widely used in learning analytics research for learner profiling
Output
Each student receives a
cluster label
.
Example clusters:
Cluster 1
High engagement + high writing scores
→
Strategic writers
Cluster 2
High engagement + low writing scores
→
Effortful but struggling writers
Cluster 3
Low engagement + low writing scores
→
At-risk learners
Cluster 4
Low engagement + moderate writing scores
→
Minimalist performers
Theoretical justification
Learning analytics research emphasises the importance of
detecting learner behaviour patterns
to support targeted pedagogical intervention.
Reference
Siemens (2013) — Learning analytics as a discipline.
2. Writing Performance Prediction Using Random Forest
Objective
Predict expected writing performance and identify the
most influential predictors of writing success
.
Data Inputs
Behavioural indicators:
time-on-task
revision frequency
feedback views
help-seeking messages
rubric consultation
Text indicators:
lexical diversity
cohesion index
average sentence length
word count
error density
Target variable:
rubric total score
Algorithm
Random Forest Regressor
Why Random Forest
Random Forest is selected because it:
models
non-linear relationships
handles
moderate educational datasets
reduces overfitting
provides
feature importance analysis
Output
The model produces:
Predicted writing score
Feature importance ranking
Example:
Feature importance results:
revision frequency → 0.28
time-on-task → 0.22
cohesion index → 0.18
lexical diversity → 0.16
This helps identify
which behaviours most strongly influence writing outcomes
.
Theoretical justification
Educational data mining research shows that machine learning models can detect
predictors of academic performance and engagement patterns
.
Reference
Baker &amp; Yacef (2009) — Educational Data Mining.
3. Competence Inference Using Bayesian Modelling
Objective
Infer
latent writing competencies
that cannot be directly observed.
Instead of evaluating only final scores, the system estimates the probability that a learner possesses specific writing skills.
Latent competencies
The Bayesian network should include:
Argument Structure Competence
Cohesion and Coherence Competence
Linguistic Complexity
Self-Regulated Revision
Each competency has three states:
Low
Medium
High
Observable evidence
Observable indicators feed the Bayesian model.
Argument Structure Competence
→ rubric argument score
→ word count
→ help-seeking messages
Cohesion Competence
→ cohesion index
→ organization score
Linguistic Complexity
→ lexical diversity
→ sentence length
Self-Regulated Revision
→ revision frequency
→ feedback views
→ time-on-task
Bayesian inference process
Step 1
Assign prior probabilities.
Example:
Argument competence
Low = 0.3
Medium = 0.5
High = 0.2
Step 2
Observe evidence from the learner.
Example:
revision frequency = high
cohesion index = moderate
argument score = moderate
Step 3
Update probabilities using Bayes’ theorem.
The model estimates the probability that the learner has
high, medium, or low competence
.
Output
Example:
Argument competence probability:
Low = 0.18
Medium = 0.54
High = 0.28
These probabilities guide adaptive feedback.
Theoretical justification
Bayesian modelling allows
probabilistic reasoning about learning under uncertainty
.
Reference
Murphy (2022) — Probabilistic Machine Learning.
Educational applications:
Baker (2010) — Data mining in education.
4. Integration with Adaptive Feedback
The final adaptive feedback engine must integrate outputs from:
Rule-based pedagogical interpretation
Clustering learner profile
Random Forest prediction
Bayesian competence inference
Example adaptive decision:
Student profile
Cluster = Effortful but struggling
Random Forest prediction
Score predicted = 22/35
Bayesian inference
Argument competence = Medium
Cohesion competence = Low
Triggered rules
Discourse organization problem
Weak revision behaviour
Adaptive response:
Strategic feedback on cohesion
Metacognitive revision prompt
Onsite paragraph restructuring guidance
5. Final System Workflow
The engineer must implement the following pipeline:
Moodle Behaviour Data
↓
Text Feature Extraction
↓
Threshold Evaluation
↓
Rule-Based Pedagogical Interpretation
↓
Learner Clustering
↓
Random Forest Performance Prediction
↓
Bayesian Competence Inference
↓
Adaptive Feedback Generation
↓
Onsite Instructional Intervention

For your dissertation, this
four-layer architecture
is methodologically strong:

This combination is powerful because it integrates:

into
