ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
As your supervisor, I would refine and
slightly reorganize the analytical strategy
so that it is
methodologically strong, defensible in a doctoral defense, and not unnecessarily complex
. Your goal is not to use as many algorithms as possible; it is to
align analysis with the research questions and theoretical framework
. In your case, the framework integrates
learning analytics, writing assessment, self-regulated learning, and adaptive feedback
, so the analyses must reflect those constructs.
Below is the
recommended analytical architecture
. It includes the
essential analyses
, removes redundancy, and clarifies
why each method is needed
.
1. Descriptive Learning Analytics (Essential)
Purpose
To
describe learner behaviour and engagement patterns
during the writing process.
Data analysed
platform logins
assignment views
rubric consultation
resource access
time-on-task
draft submissions
revision frequency
feedback views
help-seeking messages
Analyses
means and standard deviations
frequency distributions
time-series patterns across drafts
Why it is necessary
Learning analytics begins with
descriptive evidence of learner behaviour
. Without this stage, later modelling lacks interpretability.
Theoretical justification
Learning analytics focuses on
measurement and analysis of learner traces to optimize learning processes
.
Reference
Siemens (2013) – Learning analytics.
2. Writing Product Analysis (Essential)
Purpose
To measure
writing quality development across drafts
.
Data analysed
Analytic rubric scores:
task achievement
organization
argumentation
cohesion
lexical resource
grammatical accuracy
academic style
Analyses
rubric score distributions
pre- vs post-comparison
criterion-level improvement
Why it is necessary
Your study investigates
academic writing development
, so product quality must be measured independently of behavioural engagement.
Theoretical justification
Analytic scoring allows
diagnosis of specific linguistic and rhetorical weaknesses
.
Reference
Weigle (2002) – Assessing Writing.
3. Text Analytics (Essential)
Purpose
To quantify
objective textual development indicators
.
Indicators
word count
lexical diversity (TTR)
cohesion index
syntactic complexity
error density
Analyses
descriptive statistics
draft-to-draft change
Why it is necessary
Rubrics measure
pedagogical constructs
, while text analytics measures
linguistic evidence
. Combining both strengthens validity.
Theoretical justification
Computational text indicators provide empirical evidence of writing development.
Reference
Crossley &amp; McNamara – computational discourse analysis.
4. Correlation Analysis (Strongly Recommended)
Purpose
To test
relationships between behavioural engagement and writing outcomes
.
Example relationships
revision frequency ↔ writing score
time-on-task ↔ lexical diversity
feedback views ↔ score improvement
help-seeking ↔ argument development
Analyses
Pearson or Spearman correlation.
Why it is necessary
This analysis provides
empirical evidence that behaviour influences writing development
, which is central to your thesis.
Theoretical justification
Learning analytics research investigates
links between engagement patterns and performance outcomes
.
Reference
Gašević et al. (2015).
5. Learner Profiling (Clustering)
Purpose
To identify
types of writers based on behavioural and performance patterns
.
Algorithm
K-Means clustering.
Variables
time-on-task
revision frequency
feedback engagement
lexical diversity
cohesion index
rubric score
Output
Learner profiles such as:
strategic writers
effortful but struggling writers
disengaged learners
efficient minimalists
Why it is necessary
Clustering reveals
heterogeneity among learners
, enabling adaptive instruction.
Theoretical justification
Learning analytics frequently uses clustering to detect
engagement patterns
.
Reference
Dawson (2018).
6. Predictive Modelling (Random Forest)
Purpose
To predict
writing performance and improvement
.
Target variables
rubric total score
score gain between drafts
Predictor variables
revision frequency
time-on-task
feedback engagement
lexical diversity
cohesion index
Why Random Forest is appropriate
It:
handles nonlinear relationships
works well with moderate datasets
provides variable importance
Output
predicted performance
ranking of influential variables
Theoretical justification
Educational data mining uses machine learning to
predict learning outcomes from behavioural data
.
Reference
Baker &amp; Yacef (2009).
7. Bayesian Competence Modelling
Purpose
To infer
latent writing competencies
.
Latent constructs
argument competence
discourse cohesion competence
linguistic complexity
self-regulated revision
Observable evidence
rubric scores
text features
revision behaviour
feedback engagement
Output
Probability of competence levels:
low
medium
high
Why it is necessary
Bayesian modelling allows
dynamic inference of skills rather than simple scoring
.
Theoretical justification
Bayesian networks model learning processes under uncertainty.
Reference
Murphy (2022) – Probabilistic Machine Learning.
8. Growth Analysis (Highly Recommended)
Purpose
To measure
development across drafts and tasks
.
Indicators
rubric score gain
error reduction
cohesion improvement
lexical diversity growth
Analysis
paired comparison or repeated measures analysis.
Why it is necessary
Your thesis evaluates
the effectiveness of adaptive feedback
, so improvement must be demonstrated empirically.
Theoretical justification
Formative assessment research evaluates
learning progression over time
.
Reference
Black &amp; Wiliam (2011).
9. Adaptive Feedback Validation
Purpose
To verify that the adaptive feedback mechanism works.
Analysis
Compare improvement patterns between:
students receiving targeted adaptive feedback
students receiving minimal feedback
Indicators
rubric score improvement
revision depth
cohesion development
Why it is necessary
This analysis demonstrates
the pedagogical impact of the adaptive system
.
Theoretical justification
Feedback effectiveness depends on actionable guidance.
Reference
Hattie &amp; Timperley (2007).

Below is a
detailed conceptual framework
suitable for a
doctoral thesis in Applied Linguistics / Educational Technology
, aligned with your study:
“Adaptive Blended Assessment Through Learning Analytics to Enhance Academic Writing.”
The framework integrates
learning analytics, writing assessment, self-regulated learning, and adaptive feedback
within a single explanatory model.
1. Conceptual Foundation of the Study
The conceptual framework is grounded in four complementary theoretical domains that explain how academic writing development can be monitored and enhanced through adaptive assessment supported by learning analytics.
These domains include:
Second Language Academic Writing Development
Self-Regulated Learning Theory
Learning Analytics and Educational Data Mining
Formative and Adaptive Feedback
Together, these perspectives support the design of a system in which
learner behaviour, writing performance, and interaction patterns are analysed to generate adaptive instructional feedback
.
2. Core Constructs of the Framework
The model conceptualises academic writing development as an interaction between
process variables, product variables, learner regulation behaviours, and adaptive pedagogical intervention
.
The framework includes five major constructs.
3. Construct 1: Writing Process Engagement
Writing development is influenced by the
cognitive and behavioural processes learners engage in during writing tasks
.
These processes can be captured through
learning analytics indicators extracted from the Moodle learning management system
.
Process Indicators
Planning behaviour
rubric consultation
assignment access
resource consultation
time before first writing action
Drafting engagement
time-on-task
keystroke activity
word production rate
draft creation
Revision behaviour
number of revisions
depth of revisions
delay between feedback and revision
Feedback interaction
number of feedback views
revision after feedback
Help-seeking communication
private messages to instructor
clarification requests
feedback interpretation requests
Theoretical grounding
Writing process theory conceptualises writing as a recursive cognitive activity involving planning, translating ideas into text, and revising.
Reference
Flower &amp; Hayes (1981)
Self-regulated learning theory explains how learners monitor and regulate their cognitive processes during task performance.
Reference
Zimmerman (2000)
4. Construct 2: Writing Product Quality
Academic writing performance is measured through
analytic rubric assessment and computational textual indicators
.
Rubric Dimensions
Task achievement
Organization and coherence
Argument development
Lexical resource
Grammatical accuracy
Academic style
Mechanics
Textual Analytics Indicators
Word count
Lexical diversity (Type–Token Ratio)
Cohesion index (logical connectors)
Syntactic complexity
Error density
Theoretical grounding
Analytic writing assessment allows evaluators to diagnose specific dimensions of writing competence rather than relying on holistic judgement.
Reference
Weigle (2002)
Academic discourse competence involves controlling rhetorical structure, argumentation, and linguistic resources.
Reference
Hyland (2019)
5. Construct 3: Self-Regulated Writing Behaviour
Writing development is strongly influenced by learners’ ability to regulate their own writing processes.
Self-regulated writing includes:
Goal setting before writing
Monitoring during drafting
Revising based on feedback
Reflecting after task completion
Observable indicators
Revision frequency
Feedback uptake
Time allocation during writing
Help-seeking behaviour
Reflection activities
Theoretical grounding
Self-regulated learning theory proposes that effective learners regulate their learning through a cyclical process consisting of:
Forethought phase
Performance phase
Self-reflection phase
Reference
Zimmerman (2000)
Help-seeking behaviour is also considered a form of adaptive self-regulation when learners actively request clarification or assistance.
6. Construct 4: Learning Analytics Processing
Learning analytics provides the analytical infrastructure that transforms raw educational data into interpretable patterns.
Learning analytics techniques applied in this study include:
Descriptive statistics
Correlation analysis
Learner clustering
Predictive modelling
Bayesian competence inference
Purpose
These techniques allow researchers to:
identify behavioural engagement patterns
detect relationships between engagement and writing performance
predict writing outcomes
infer latent competencies
Theoretical grounding
Learning analytics is defined as the measurement and analysis of learner data for the purpose of understanding and optimising learning processes.
Reference
Siemens (2013)
Educational data mining techniques are used to model learner behaviour and performance.
Reference
Baker &amp; Yacef (2009)
7. Construct 5: Adaptive Feedback and Instructional Intervention
The final component of the conceptual framework is the
adaptive feedback system
, which translates learning analytics insights into targeted pedagogical responses.
Adaptive feedback is generated based on:
performance indicators
engagement patterns
identified learner profiles
probabilistic competence inference
Types of feedback
Direct corrective feedback
Indirect corrective feedback
Strategic feedback on argumentation and structure
Metacognitive prompts
Feedforward guidance
Dialogic onsite feedback
Instructional responses
Paragraph restructuring support
Argument development scaffolds
Grammar mini-lessons
Revision workshops
Feedback interpretation sessions
Theoretical grounding
Effective feedback answers three key questions:
Where am I going?
How am I doing?
What should I do next?
Reference
Hattie &amp; Timperley (2007)
Feedback literacy research also emphasises learners’ ability to interpret and act upon feedback.
Reference
Carless &amp; Boud (2018)
8. Integration of Constructs
The conceptual framework proposes that
academic writing development emerges from the interaction between learner engagement, writing performance, and adaptive instructional support
.
The model assumes that:
Behavioural engagement patterns influence writing performance.
Writing process data and textual indicators provide evidence of underlying writing competencies.
Learning analytics techniques can identify patterns and predict outcomes.
Adaptive feedback informed by analytics can guide learners toward improved writing performance.
9. Analytical Mechanisms Within the Framework
The framework operationalises the constructs through a sequence of analytical processes.
Stage 1: Behavioural Analysis
Descriptive learning analytics examine engagement patterns.
Stage 2: Writing Quality Analysis
Rubric scores and text analytics evaluate writing performance.
Stage 3: Relationship Analysis
Correlation analysis identifies relationships between behavioural engagement and writing outcomes.
Stage 4: Learner Profiling
Clustering techniques identify learner engagement profiles.
Stage 5: Predictive Modelling
Random Forest algorithms predict writing performance based on behavioural and textual indicators.
Stage 6: Competence Inference
Bayesian networks estimate latent writing competencies.
Stage 7: Adaptive Intervention
Adaptive feedback and onsite instructional support are generated based on diagnostic insights.
10. Conceptual Model of the Study
The conceptual framework can be summarised as a multi-layered system.
Learner Behaviour
(Moodle logs, revision patterns, help-seeking)
↓
Writing Process Indicators
(time-on-task, drafts, feedback interaction)
↓
Writing Product Indicators
(rubric scores, textual analytics)
↓
Learning Analytics Processing
(descriptive analysis, correlations, clustering)
↓
Machine Learning Modelling
(Random Forest prediction)
↓
Competence Inference
(Bayesian network modelling)
↓
Adaptive Feedback Engine
(data-driven feedback selection)
↓
Onsite Instructional Intervention
(targeted pedagogical support)
↓
Writing Improvement Across Drafts
11. Expected Outcomes of the Framework
The conceptual framework predicts that:
Students with higher behavioural engagement will demonstrate greater writing improvement.
Revision behaviour will positively correlate with improvements in argumentation and cohesion.
Learner profiles identified through clustering will exhibit distinct writing development patterns.
Adaptive feedback informed by analytics will lead to measurable improvements in writing performance.
12. Contribution of the Framework
The framework contributes to the field by integrating:
learning analytics
writing assessment
self-regulated learning theory
adaptive feedback design
into a
data-driven model of academic writing development in EFL contexts
.
This integration allows writing instruction to move beyond traditional evaluation toward
diagnostic and adaptive learning support
.

lahmarabbou asmaalahmarabbou asmaa - Sat, 7 Mar 2026, 11:02 PM
Another big worry about intelligence in education is how it affects fairness and relationships between students.
When some students use AI tools a lot to do their assignments while others do their work on their own it can make school unfair.Students who take time to research write and revise their work might feel discouraged when they see others getting better results with little effort by using AI.This can make classmates not trust each other and make students less motivated to work
Over time it can also cause problems in the classroom because students start to think that doing well in school is not about trying or being good at something.
For instance a study at Harvard University found that some students who used AI tools in a way got higher grades than their friends who did their work without AI help (Harvard University, 2023).These unfair situations can hurt how well students work together because they might not want to share ideas or help each other much.Also relying much on AI can hurt the development of key skills, like critical thinking solving problems and perseverance.So universities should make rules that guide the responsible use of AI to protect fairness and keep school honest.

5. Data preparation before clustering
Before clustering, variables must be

because they are on different scales.
Example:
Variable
Range
Assignment views
0–20
Word count
0–300
Error density
0–15
Normalization ensures that no variable dominates the clustering.
Standard method
Z = (x − mean) / standard deviation
This is called
z-score standardization
.
6. Clustering algorithm
Your study should use:

Reason:
simple
widely used in learning analytics
effective for behavioural pattern detection
interpretable clusters
Academic support

7. Determining number of clusters
Use the
Elbow Method
.
Procedure:
Run K-means for different numbers of clusters
Calculate
within-cluster variance
Select the point where improvement stabilizes
Typical result for writing studies:
3 clusters
Cluster types usually become:
1️⃣ High engagement – high performance
2️⃣ High engagement – developing writers
3️⃣ Low engagement – struggling writers

1. Purpose of Random Forest in your study
Random Forest is used to
predict writing performance and identify the factors that most influence writing improvement
.
Unlike clustering (which groups learners), Random Forest is a
supervised machine learning model
.
It answers two key research questions:
1️⃣
Can behavioural and textual indicators predict writing quality?
2️⃣
Which variables most strongly influence improvement in academic writing?
2. Why Random Forest is appropriate for your thesis
Random Forest is particularly suitable for educational data because:
it handles
nonlinear relationships
it works well with
moderate datasets
it is robust to
noise and missing data
it provides
feature importance
, which explains which factors influence outcomes.
Theoretical justification
Random Forest is widely used in
educational data mining
.
Relevant references:
Baker &amp; Inventado (2014)
— Educational Data Mining Handbook
Romero &amp; Ventura (2020)
— Educational data mining methods
Gašević et al. (2017)
— Learning analytics and engagement research
These studies show Random Forest models effectively predict
learning performance from behavioural indicators
.

, explained in a way that is both

1. Purpose of Bayesian modelling in your study
Bayesian modelling is used to
infer latent writing competencies
that cannot be directly observed.
In writing assessment, we observe:
behavioural data (Moodle logs)
writing process data (revision behaviour)
product data (rubric scores, text features)
However, the real constructs we want to estimate are
underlying competencies
, such as:
argumentation competence
discourse cohesion competence
linguistic control
self-regulated revision ability
These are
latent variables
.
Bayesian modelling allows the system to
estimate the probability that a student possesses these competencies
, given the observed evidence.
2. Why Bayesian modelling is theoretically appropriate
Bayesian models are widely used in
learning analytics and intelligent tutoring systems
because they allow:
probabilistic inference
integration of multiple evidence sources
dynamic updating when new data arrive
Theoretical grounding
Key foundations include:
Mislevy et al. (2003)
– Evidence-Centered Design for assessment
Pearl (1988)
– Probabilistic reasoning and Bayesian networks
Murphy (2022)
– Probabilistic machine learning
Baker &amp; Inventado (2014)
– educational data mining methods
These frameworks argue that
student ability must be inferred probabilistically from multiple indicators
.
3. Structure of the Bayesian model in your study
The model contains
two layers
.
Layer 1 — latent competencies
These represent the
student’s underlying writing skills
.
Latent Competence
Description
Argument Structure
ability to construct and support claims
Cohesion &amp; Coherence
ability to link ideas logically
Linguistic Complexity
grammatical and lexical control
Self-Regulated Revision
ability to revise and improve drafts
Each competence has three possible states:
Low
Medium
High
Layer 2 — observable evidence
These are
measurable indicators extracted from your analytics system
.

Data Source

Example relationships:

Self-regulated revision → Revision frequency
Self-regulated revision → Feedback interaction
Graphically:

↓

.

7. Bayesian updating process
When evidence is observed, the model updates probabilities using
Bayes’ theorem
.
Conceptually:
Posterior Probability =
Likelihood × Prior
--------------------
Evidence probability
This produces
posterior competence estimates
.

9. Why Bayesian inference is powerful
Unlike simple scoring, Bayesian modelling:
integrates multiple evidence sources
handles uncertainty
updates predictions when new drafts appear

.

1. Purpose of the Rule-Based Interpretation Layer

.

. ETS.

Your rules implement exactly this structure.

Product	Rubric Organization ≤ 2 and Cohesion Index low	Discourse organization problem	Strategic feedback on paragraph unity, transitions, and logical sequencing	Analytic writing assessment supports criterion-level diagnosis, while L2 writing pedagogy treats cohesion and organization as central components of academic discourse competence.
Product	Rubric Argumentation ≤ 2	Weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure	Higher-order feedback on argument development, examples, and explanation	Analytic scoring isolates specific writing weaknesses, and academic writing instruction emphasizes explicit development of argument and support.
Product	High Error Density with Grammar score ≤ 2	Linguistic accuracy problem	Direct or metalinguistic corrective feedback plus focused practice	Analytic assessment enables diagnosis of form-level problems; corrective feedback is appropriate when error patterns are repeated and systematic.

Process	Help-seeking message after feedback	Emerging adaptive self-regulation	Dialogic scaffolding and clarification support	Help-seeking reflects monitoring and adaptive response to difficulty, consistent with SRL-based feedback models.
Engagement	Low assignment views + low resource access + low submissions	Disengagement / low behavioural participation	Motivational reminder, task clarification, reduced task complexity if necessary	Learning analytics uses behavioural trace data to optimize learning; low participation is a key early-warning indicator.

3. Threshold specification to encode in the tool
Thresholds must be implemented as provisional pedagogical decision bands, not universal psychometric cut-scores. They are justified by task expectations, analytic rubric logic, self-regulated learning theory, feedback literacy, and pilot calibration. For this study, the reference task is an argumentative body paragraph expected to be approximately 120-150 words and produced in roughly 30-40 minutes.
Indicator	Low / risk	Moderate / watch	Strong / satisfactory	Operational meaning	Primary justification
Word count	&lt; 80	80-119	120-150 (or above if task allows)	Underdevelopment vs adequate elaboration for a body paragraph	Task-based expectation; Hyland (2019)
Time on task (minutes)	&lt; 15	15-29	30-40+	Insufficient engagement vs adequate composing time	Zimmerman (2000) forethought/performance
Rubric views	0	1	&gt;= 2	Low criteria awareness vs active consultation	Assessment literacy; Nicol &amp; Macfarlane-Dick (2006)
Revision frequency	0	1	&gt;= 2	No monitoring vs limited revision vs sustained revision	Zimmerman (2000); Hayes (2012)
Error density (per 100 words)	&gt; 8	4-8	&lt; 4	High recurrent form-level difficulty vs manageable error load	Weigle (2002); corrective feedback logic
Cohesion index (per paragraph)	&lt; 2	2-3	&gt;= 4	Weak discourse linking vs developing cohesion vs strong linking	Hyland (2019)
Criterion scores (1-5)	1-2	3	4-5	Clear weakness vs developing control vs relative strength	Analytic rubric logic; Weigle (2002)
Feedback uptake	feedback viewed, no revision	feedback viewed, minor revision	feedback viewed, substantive revision	Exposure only vs partial use vs active use of feedback	Carless &amp; Boud (2018)
Help-seeking messages	0 when performance is weak	1 procedural message	&gt;= 1 clarification/argument/language message	No help seeking vs minimal procedural help vs adaptive self-regulation	Zimmerman (2000)
Threshold rationale the engineer must preserve
1. Thresholds are task-sensitive. The tool must allow the researcher to edit expected word range and expected time range by task. 2. Criterion score bands of 1-2, 3, and 4-5 follow the logic of analytic writing assessment, where low scores identify dimensions needing support, 3 indicates developing performance, and 4-5 indicates relative strength. 3. Process thresholds must remain editable because pilot data may require recalibration by quartiles, medians, or empirically observed distributions.
4. Interpretation rules the tool must apply
The engineer must implement the following rules as editable decision logic. Each rule maps a condition to a pedagogical interpretation, a feedback type, and an onsite instructional intervention. The tool must save the triggered rule IDs for every student and draft.
Rule ID	Condition to detect	Pedagogical interpretation	Adaptive feedback type	Onsite intervention	Theoretical basis
A1	rubric_views = 0 and first_access_delay high	Weak forethought regulation and low criteria awareness	Criteria-referenced prompt + planning scaffold	Rubric walkthrough + model outline	Zimmerman (2000); Nicol &amp; Macfarlane-Dick (2006)
A2	time_on_task &lt; 15 and word_count &lt; 80	Insufficient planning and underdeveloped task engagement	Elaboration prompt + staged drafting prompt	Guided planning and idea expansion support	Zimmerman (2000); Hyland (2019)
B1	organization &lt;= 2 and cohesion_index &lt; 2	Discourse organization problem	Strategic feedback on paragraph structure and transitions	Paragraph restructuring with model paragraph	Hyland (2019); Weigle (2002)
B2	argumentation &lt;= 2	Weak claim-evidence-reasoning structure	Higher-order feedback on support and explanation	Claim-evidence-explanation scaffold	Hyland (2019); Weigle (2002)
B3	grammar_accuracy &lt;= 2 and error_density &gt; 8	Persistent linguistic accuracy problem	Direct corrective or metalinguistic feedback	Focused mini-lesson on recurrent errors	Weigle (2002); corrective feedback tradition
B4	lexical_resource &lt;= 2 and ttr low	Restricted lexical repertoire	Lexical enrichment feedback	Academic vocabulary expansion support	Hyland (2019)
C1	revision_frequency = 0	Weak self-regulated revision	Metacognitive revision prompt	Guided revision checklist	Zimmerman (2000); Hayes (2012)
C2	feedback_views &gt;= 1 and revision_frequency = 0	Low feedback uptake / weak feedback literacy	Feedforward message explaining how to use comments	Feedback decoding session	Carless &amp; Boud (2018); Hattie &amp; Timperley (2007)
C3	revision_frequency &gt;= 1 but argumentation remains &lt;= 2	Surface revision without meaning-level development	Strategic global-revision feedback	Teacher modelling of global revision moves	Hayes (2012); Flower &amp; Hayes (1981)
D1	help_seeking_messages &gt;= 1 and message type in {feedback clarification, argument help, language help}	Adaptive help-seeking	Dialogic scaffolding	Short clarification conference	Zimmerman (2000); Carless &amp; Boud (2018)
D2	performance weak and help_seeking_messages = 0	Passive disengagement or unrecognized difficulty	Motivational + metacognitive prompt	Proactive teacher check-in	Siemens (2013); Zimmerman (2000)
5. Modelling layer beyond rules
The tool must include three complementary AI/analytics methods. These do not replace the rule layer; they extend it by profiling learners, predicting outcomes, and inferring latent competence.
Method	Variables to use	What the tool must output	Why it is included	Primary justification
Learner clustering (K-means)	time_on_task; revision_frequency; feedback_views; rubric_views; help_seeking_messages; total_score; ttr; cohesion_index; word_count	Cluster label for each student/draft and cluster centroids	To identify learner profiles such as strategic writers, effortful but struggling writers, disengaged learners, and minimalist performers	Learning analytics pattern detection; Siemens (2013); Dawson (2018)
Random Forest prediction	Behavioural + product + text variables; target = total_score or score_gain	Predicted score or gain; feature importance ranking	To detect the strongest predictors of writing quality/growth and support adaptive prioritization	Educational data mining; Baker &amp; Yacef (2009)
Bayesian competence inference	Observable evidence linked to latent nodes: argumentation, cohesion, linguistic complexity, self-regulated revision	Posterior probabilities for low/medium/high competence	To infer skill states under uncertainty rather than relying on observed scores alone	Probabilistic modelling; Murphy (2022); Mislevy (2003)

8. How the system should work in the actual intervention
The operational logic should be described as follows:
Student completes writing task in Moodle
↓
Process and product data are collected
↓
Indicators are thresholded
↓
Rule-based pedagogical interpretations are triggered
↓
Learner is profiled through clustering
↓

↓

↓

↓

9. Methodological coherence of the model
Your concern is exactly right: the four major components must not appear as parallel elements. They must function as one system.
The correct integration is:
Academic writing assessment
Defines the
product constructs
:
argumentation
cohesion
lexical control
grammar
organization
Learning analytics
Defines the
process constructs
:
engagement
planning
revision
feedback use
help-seeking
Adaptive pedagogy
Defines the
instructional response logic
:
rule-based interpretation
threshold-triggered support
onsite intervention
AI / Bayesian modelling
Extends the system by:
profiling learners
predicting growth
inferring latent competence
So these are not four separate components. They are
four layers of one adaptive writing assessment architecture
.
10. Final methodological recommendation
To make the model fully defensible, keep these principles explicit:
Thresholds are provisional and task-sensitive
Rules are pedagogical, transparent, and editable
Clustering is used for profiling, not diagnosis alone
Random Forest is used for prediction and variable importance
Bayesian modelling is used for competence inference under uncertainty
All advanced modelling must remain subordinate to the pedagogical logic of the study
That last point is essential. The thesis is not about using AI for its own sake. It is about
using analytics and modelling to support adaptive writing instruction
.

At the present stage, the main methodological challenge is to ensure that academic writing assessment, learning analytics, adaptive pedagogy, and AI/Bayesian modelling are not treated as parallel elements, but as interdependent layers of a single adaptive assessment architecture. The key task is therefore to maintain a clear analytical sequence through which pre-test diagnosis is transformed into learner profiling, competence inference, and pedagogically meaningful feedback decisions.
That wording is precise and doctoral-level.

Random Forest
is useful if you want to show that behavioural and textual indicators can
predict writing performance or improvement
. It is strong for the AI component, but again it is
optional unless prediction is one of your central research aims
.
Bayesian modelling
is the most conceptually ambitious. It is valuable only if you truly want to infer
latent competencies
such as argumentation, cohesion, and self-regulated revision from multiple evidence sources. It is powerful, but it is also the easiest way to overextend the thesis if not handled carefully.
As your supervisor, I would say:
Essential:
descriptive analytics, rubric/product analysis, text analytics, correlation analysis, growth analysis, rule-based adaptive feedback
Strongly recommended:
clustering
Optional but valuable:
Random Forest
Most optional / only if well controlled:
Bayesian modelling
So,
no
, all three are not strictly required.
For a strong and defensible thesis, you could do very well with:
descriptive analytics + product analysis + text analytics + correlation + clustering + rule-based adaptive feedback
Then add
Random Forest
only if you want a stronger AI layer, and add
Bayesian modelling
only if you are fully confident you can keep it simple and tightly connected to your research questions.

ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
In your study, the process works like a cycle that follows the student step by step. First, the student writes the paragraph in Moodle, and the system collects both
process data
(how they worked, such as time, revisions, and feedback use) and
product data
(the quality of the text). Then, these indicators are compared to predefined thresholds to see whether the performance is low, moderate, or strong. Based on this, the system applies clear rules to interpret what the student’s difficulties are (for example, weak argument or poor revision). After that, the system groups the student into a learner profile using clustering, predicts their performance using Random Forest, and estimates their underlying skills using Bayesian modelling. All this information is then used to generate personalized feedback and guide the teacher’s onsite support. The student revises the text, and finally, the system measures improvement across drafts to evaluate whether learning has actually occurred.

ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
The process in your study works as a structured cycle that transforms raw data into personalized learning support. First, the student writes the paragraph in Moodle, and the system collects
process data
(such as time-on-task, revisions, feedback views) and
product data
(rubric scores and text features). These indicators are then compared to
thresholds
(for example, low word count, high error density, or no revision) to determine whether the student’s performance is weak, developing, or strong. After that,
rule-based interpretation
is applied: specific conditions (e.g., low argument score or no revision) are translated into clear pedagogical diagnoses (such as weak reasoning or poor self-regulation). The system then builds a more complete learner profile through clustering, predicts likely performance using Random Forest, and estimates underlying competencies using Bayesian modelling. Based on all these layers, the system generates
personalized feedback
tailored to each student’s needs—for example, focusing on argument development, cohesion, or revision strategies rather than giving generic comments. The teacher then provides targeted onsite support, the student revises their work, and finally, improvement is measured across drafts to confirm whether the feedback led to actual learning.

Thought for 9m 2s
That sentence expresses a
two-stage methodological logic
:
first, you define thresholds from theory and task design; second, you refine them using real data from your own context.
This is important because it shows your thresholds are
neither arbitrary nor purely statistical
. They are
pedagogically meaningful
from the start, but also
empirically adjusted
so they fit your learners and your intervention.
What “thresholds” means in your study
A threshold is a
decision point
used to classify an indicator as weak, developing, or strong.
For example:
word count below a certain level may indicate
underdeveloped writing
no revision may indicate
weak self-regulated revision
low cohesion may indicate a
discourse organization problem
feedback viewed without revision may indicate
weak feedback uptake
So thresholds are the mechanism that turns raw numbers into
instructional signals
.
Without thresholds, “198 words,” “1 revision,” or “0 rubric views” are just numbers.
With thresholds, they become diagnoses such as:
adequate elaboration
limited revision behaviour
low criteria awareness
Part 1: “Thresholds were initially defined…”
This means you do
not wait for the data first
and then invent your cut-points.
You begin by defining them
before analysis
, based on the logic of the task and the literature.
This protects your study from being seen as arbitrary or post hoc.
In other words, you begin with a
theory-informed framework
.
Part 2: “…based on task expectations…”
This means each threshold must make sense for the
specific writing task
students are completing.
In your case, the task is not a full essay. It is an
argumentative body paragraph
.
That matters because the expected performance is shaped by the task.
For example, a body paragraph should usually contain:
a topic sentence
a supporting point
an example or evidence
an explanation
some cohesion
So if a student writes only 50 or 60 words, that is probably too short to perform the full rhetorical function of the task. That is why a threshold like:
&lt; 80 words = underdeveloped
120–150 words = adequate
is not random. It is based on what the task structurally requires.
So “task expectations” means:
a threshold is justified by what the student is supposed to do in that specific assignment.
This is why thresholds should remain
task-sensitive
, not fixed forever.
Part 3: “…analytic rubric structure…”
This means your thresholds also come from the internal logic of your
rubric
.
Your rubric uses criterion scores such as:
1–2 = weak
3 = developing
4–5 = strong
That scale already provides a built-in threshold structure.
So if a student gets:
Argumentation = 2
, you interpret that as a real weakness
Cohesion = 3
, you interpret that as developing but not yet strong
Grammar = 4
, you interpret that as relatively controlled
This is important because analytic writing assessment is designed specifically to separate writing into
diagnosable dimensions
. That is exactly what you need for adaptive feedback.
So “analytic rubric structure” means:
your thresholds are aligned with the scoring architecture of the assessment instrument itself.
This is methodologically strong because the threshold is not imported from nowhere. It is embedded in the rubric design.
Part 4: “…and theoretical models of self-regulated learning and academic writing.”
This is where you justify
process thresholds
and
writing-performance thresholds
conceptually.
A. Self-regulated learning theory
Self-regulated learning, especially in Zimmerman’s model, explains that effective learners move through phases such as:
forethought
performance
self-reflection
From this theory, you can justify thresholds such as:
late first access + very short time-on-task
= weak forethought regulation
no revision
= weak monitoring and control
help-seeking after difficulty
= adaptive self-regulation
feedback viewed without revision
= limited feedback uptake
These thresholds are not just numerical. They represent theoretically meaningful patterns of learner behaviour.
So when you classify:
revision frequency = 0 as weak
revision frequency ≥ 2 as stronger regulation
you are relying on the idea that revision is a sign of monitoring and control in the writing process.
B. Academic writing theory
Writing theory justifies thresholds related to text quality.
For example:
low cohesion index may indicate weak discourse control
low lexical diversity may indicate restricted vocabulary use
argument score ≤ 2 may indicate weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure
high error density may indicate persistent linguistic difficulty
These are based on what academic writing is understood to involve:
elaboration
coherence
support
lexical precision
grammatical control
So this part of the sentence means:
your thresholds are theoretically meaningful because they represent recognised dimensions of writing and learning regulation.
Part 5: “These thresholds were subsequently refined…”
This means your thresholds are
not frozen permanently
after the theoretical stage.
After you define them, you test them against your
actual student data
.
That is where refinement begins.
This is important because theory gives you an intelligent starting point, but your real students may behave differently from what the literature suggests in abstract terms.
For instance:
maybe most of your students write 160–190 words for a body paragraph
maybe almost nobody revises more than once
maybe the “strong” time-on-task band needs to be lower or higher in your context
So refinement means:
you check whether your initial thresholds actually work in practice.
Part 6: “…through empirical calibration…”
This is the technical part.
Empirical calibration means that you use
observed student performance data
to verify whether your thresholds are realistic, discriminating, and useful.
This does
not
mean data replaces theory. It means data is used to
adjust
theory-based thresholds.
For example:
Example 1: word count
You initially define:
&lt; 80 = low
80–119 = moderate
120–150 = strong
Then you examine your pilot data and find that nearly all students wrote between 145 and 190 words.
That may suggest your “strong” category is too narrow or that the task naturally elicits longer writing. You may then refine the bands.
Example 2: time-on-task
You initially define:
&lt; 15 min = low
15–29 = moderate
30+ = strong
Then you discover that most students produce acceptable paragraphs in about 22–27 minutes because they draft offline first or are already familiar with the task type.
That means your threshold may need contextual adjustment.
Example 3: revision frequency
You may initially treat:
0 = low
1 = moderate
2+ = strong
But if your course structure only gives limited time for revision, then one revision may actually be pedagogically meaningful in context.
So empirical calibration means checking whether the thresholds:
match real learner behaviour
distinguish students meaningfully
correspond to visible differences in writing quality
Part 7: “…using observed student performance data…”
This means you refine thresholds using your
actual dataset
, especially from:
pilot cases
pre-test scripts
first intervention cycles
Moodle traces
draft comparisons
You look at the observed distributions and ask:
Are these thresholds too harsh?
Are they too lenient?
Do they separate strong, developing, and weak cases meaningfully?
Do they correspond to what I see in real student writing?
This is where your methodology becomes robust.
You are no longer saying:
“I think these are good thresholds.”
You are saying:
“These thresholds were initially theory-based and then tested against real student behaviour and performance.”
That is far more defensible.
Part 8: “…to ensure contextual validity.”
This is the final goal.
Contextual validity means the thresholds are appropriate for:
your students
your university
your writing tasks
your Moodle environment
your intervention design
A threshold that might make sense in one setting may not make sense in another.
For example:
a threshold developed for advanced postgraduate writers may not fit third-year EFL students
a threshold designed for full essays may not fit body paragraphs
a threshold developed in a fully online course may not fit blended learning
So contextual validity means:
your thresholds are valid not only in theory, but in your actual teaching and research context.
This is what makes your adaptive system credible.
What this means practically in your study
In practical terms, the sentence means you should do the following:
Step 1
Define thresholds
before full analysis
based on:
task requirements
rubric bands
SRL theory
writing theory
Step 2
Use them in your pilot cases and early dataset.
Step 3
Check whether they actually distinguish learners meaningfully.
Step 4
Refine them if necessary using real performance patterns.
Step 5
Document both:
the original theoretical rationale
the empirical refinement process
That is the ideal doctoral approach.
Example from your own study
Take
revision frequency
.
Initial theoretical definition
From self-regulated learning theory:
no revision indicates weak monitoring
revision indicates some regulation
So you define:
0 = low
1 = moderate
2+ = strong
Empirical calibration
Then in your pilot, you find:
most students revise only once
those who revise once still improve substantially
very few revise twice because of course timing
So you may keep the categories, but explain that:
in your context, one revision is already meaningful
thresholds remain pedagogically motivated but contextually interpreted
That is exactly what the sentence is saying.
Why this wording is methodologically strong
Because it avoids two common errors:
Error 1: purely intuitive thresholds
This is weak because it sounds arbitrary.
Error 2: purely data-driven thresholds
This is also weak in a pedagogical study because it disconnects the model from theory and task design.
Your wording avoids both by showing:
theoretical grounding
pedagogical relevance
empirical refinement
contextual appropriateness
That is the right balance.
The simplest way to remember it
You can think of the sentence like this:
Theory gives the first version of the threshold; data helps refine it.
Or even more simply:
You define thresholds with theory, then test them with real students.
A strong doctoral-level paraphrase
You could also express the same idea like this:
Thresholds were not treated as fixed or universal cut-points. Rather, they were established initially from the pedagogical demands of the task, the logic of analytic writing assessment, and theoretical expectations about self-regulated learning behaviour, then adjusted through pilot observation and actual learner performance data so that they accurately reflected the realities of the local instructional context.
What an examiner wants to hear
If an examiner asks, “Why did you choose these thresholds?”, the strongest answer is:
I first derived them from task expectations, rubric logic, self-regulated learning theory, and academic writing theory. I then checked them against my pilot and observed student data to ensure that they were meaningful and realistic in my own EFL context.
That is a very strong answer.

Stage 4: Rule-based interpretation
This is the stage where your system stops being a list of numbers and starts becoming a
pedagogical decision system
. In Stage 3, you converted raw data into threshold bands such as
low
,
moderate
, and
strong
. In Stage 4, you ask:
What does that pattern mean educationally, and what support should follow?
This logic is well aligned with Evidence-Centered Design, which links
observed evidence
to
claims about learner competence
and then to
assessment or instructional action
.
ETS
+1
What enters this stage
The input is
not raw Moodle logs
and
not the full essay by itself
. The input is the
thresholded profile
you already created, for example:
word count = strong
revision frequency = moderate
help-seeking = strong
argumentation = weak/developing
cohesion = moderate
feedback uptake = partial
So Stage 4 works on
structured signals
, not on unprocessed data. That is why thresholds must come before rules.
CRESST
+1
What a rule looks like
A rule is an
if–then pedagogical statement
:
If
certain indicators appear together,
then
the system assigns a pedagogical interpretation,
and
then
it selects a feedback response.
So a rule always has three parts:
Condition
Interpretation
Adaptive response
For example:
If
organization is low and cohesion is low,
then
interpret this as a discourse organization problem,
and
respond with strategic feedback on paragraph structure and transitions.
This rule-based logic is educationally strong because it makes the system
transparent and replicable
rather than mysterious or purely algorithmic.
ETS
+1
Why this stage is necessary
You need this layer because thresholds alone only tell you that something is weak or strong; they do
not
tell you what kind of teaching response is needed. A low argument score and a low revision frequency are both “problems,” but they are
different kinds of problems
. One needs higher-order rhetorical support; the other needs self-regulation support. Rule-based interpretation is therefore the stage that translates analytics into pedagogy. Product-side rules are justified by analytic writing assessment and L2 writing pedagogy, while process-side rules are justified by self-regulated learning and feedback literacy research.
Taylor &amp; Francis Online
+3
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+3
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+3
How you build the rules
You organize the rules into the same three dimensions you already use in the study:
1. Product rules
These interpret weaknesses in the written text itself.
Examples:
low organization + low cohesion → discourse organization problem
low argumentation → weak claim–evidence–reasoning
high error density + low grammar score → linguistic accuracy problem
low lexical diversity + low lexical score → restricted lexical repertoire
These rules are grounded in analytic assessment because writing quality must be broken into diagnosable components rather than treated as one undifferentiated score. They are also grounded in L2 writing pedagogy because organization, support, cohesion, and lexical control are central to academic writing competence.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+1
2. Process rules
These interpret how the student worked.
Examples:
no revision → weak self-regulated revision
feedback viewed but no revision follows → low feedback uptake
low rubric consultation before drafting → weak criteria awareness
short time-on-task + late first access → weak forethought regulation
help-seeking after difficulty → adaptive self-regulation
These rules are grounded in Zimmerman’s cyclical SRL model, where forethought, performance, and self-reflection are key phases, and in feedback literacy research, which treats acting on feedback as more important than merely receiving it.
Springer
+2
Taylor &amp; Francis Online
+2
3. Engagement rules
These interpret the student’s overall participation pattern.
Examples:
low views + low resource access + low submissions → disengagement
high engagement + low writing quality → effortful but struggling writer
low engagement + acceptable quality → efficient but fragile regulation
These rules matter because behavioural engagement and product quality do not always move together. A student may work hard but still struggle, or perform adequately with unstable learning habits. Learning analytics is valuable precisely because it helps detect those different participation patterns.
Springer
+1

Rule 1: argument development
Your written feedback on Draft 1 explicitly said:
“Argument present but explanation and development are limited.”
That means the pedagogical issue is not absence of a claim, but insufficient development of reasoning and support. So the rule triggered is:
If argument is present but underdeveloped, then interpret as weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure, and respond with higher-order feedback on explanation and support.
This rule is justified because argument quality in academic writing depends not only on having a position, but on developing and supporting it clearly.
Cambridge University Press &amp; Assessment
+1
Rule 2: revision after feedback
She produced a second draft, so this is
not
a no-revision case. However, the second draft improved only partially: the idea became clearer, but evidence integration, cohesion, and sentence control still remained uneven. So the appropriate interpretation is:
If revision occurs but major rhetorical weaknesses remain, then interpret this as partial feedback uptake or revision without enough meaning-level development, and respond with feedforward plus guided global revision.
This rule is methodologically sound because feedback literacy is not measured by exposure to comments alone, but by whether the learner can turn comments into effective changes.
Taylor &amp; Francis Online
+1
Rule 3: help-seeking
Her message about uncertainty over academic vocabulary is a strong example of adaptive help-seeking. She identified a difficulty, tried to solve it herself, and then requested confirmation. So the rule triggered is:
If a help-seeking message reflects clarification about language, argument, or feedback, then interpret it as adaptive self-regulation, and respond with dialogic scaffolding.
This is exactly the kind of behaviour SRL theory treats as productive monitoring and strategic control rather than dependence.

Rule 4: engagement pattern
Her process data showed repeated assignment access, resource consultation, rubric use, reflection participation, and task submission. So she does
not

If engagement is high but writing quality remains moderate, then interpret the learner as effortful but still developing, and respond with explicit strategic instruction rather than motivational remediation.
This is important because it prevents the wrong intervention. She does not need motivational pressure; she needs structured discourse support.

pedagogical diagnosis
, not just a score profile. For your student, the diagnosis becomes something like this:

this student is partially using feedback
this student is trying to regulate her language choices
this student needs higher-order support more than basic motivational intervention
That is the real pedagogical value of the system. It is also why the rule layer should remain

, even when you later add clustering, Random Forest, and Bayesian inference.

Stage 5:
AI (clustering + Random Forest + Bayesian)
This stage comes
after thresholds and rules
. By this point, the system already has a pedagogical reading of the student. The AI layer does
not replace
that reading. It adds three extra functions:
clustering
= profile the learner type
Random Forest
= predict likely performance/improvement
Bayesian modelling
= estimate hidden competence levels
So this stage answers three different questions:
What kind of learner is this student?
How likely is this student to improve or perform well?
What underlying competencies are probably strong or weak?
1. Clustering
Clustering groups students by similarity.
It does
not
use teacher comments or theory directly.
It uses the variables you selected, such as:
assignment views
resource access
rubric views
time-on-task
revision frequency
feedback views
help-seeking
word count
cohesion
total score
Then it asks:
Which students look similar across these indicators?
The system may produce groups such as:
strategic writers
engaged but developing writers
disengaged learners
minimalist performers
For your student
Based on the data you gave, Asmaa would likely fall into:
engaged but developing writer
Why?
Because she shows:
repeated access
resource use
help-seeking
revision
moderate product quality
So clustering gives her a
profile label
.
In simple words
Clustering says:
“This student belongs to this kind of learner group.”
It does not yet predict success. It just identifies the profile.
2. Random Forest
Random Forest is used for
prediction
.
It takes the same kinds of indicators and predicts an outcome, such as:
total score
score gain across drafts
probability of improvement
So it asks:
Given this student’s behaviour and writing indicators, how likely is she to improve?
It also tells you which variables matter most.
For example, the model may show that the strongest predictors are:
revision frequency
cohesion index
feedback interaction
argument score
For your student
Because Asmaa has:
strong engagement
revision present
help-seeking present
moderate writing quality
the Random Forest model would likely predict:
moderate to good improvement potential
But it would also show that her improvement may depend especially on:
argument development
cohesion
effective use of feedback
In simple words
Random Forest says:
“This student is likely to improve to this level, and these variables are the main reasons.”
So clustering gives the
profile
, but Random Forest gives the
prediction
.
3. Bayesian modelling
Bayesian modelling is used for
competence inference
.
This is different from profiling and prediction.
It asks:
What hidden writing abilities probably exist behind the visible data?
Because things like:
argumentation ability
cohesion competence
self-regulated revision
cannot be seen directly.
You only see evidence:
argument score
cohesion index
revision behaviour
help-seeking
feedback use
The Bayesian model combines that evidence and estimates probabilities like:
argumentation competence = medium
cohesion competence = low-medium
self-regulated revision = medium-high
For your student
For Asmaa, a reasonable Bayesian reading would be:
argument competence
= medium
cohesion competence
= developing
self-regulated revision
= medium-high
lexical/academic language control
= developing
Why?
Because she:
has argument present, but underdeveloped
revised after feedback
asked for vocabulary clarification
used resources actively
In simple words
Bayesian modelling says:
“Behind this student’s performance, these underlying competencies are probably at these levels.”
So it gives you a deeper diagnosis.
4. How the three AI parts work together
They do
three different jobs
:
Clustering
Profiles the learner
What type of learner is this?
Random Forest
Predicts outcome
How likely is this learner to improve?
Bayesian
Infers competence
What hidden abilities are strong or weak?
5. Apply all three to your student
For
Lahmarabbou Asmaa
, the AI layer would likely produce something like this:
Clustering output
Cluster:
engaged but developing writer
Random Forest output
Predicted improvement:
moderate/good
Most relevant factors:
revision, engagement, cohesion, argument development
Bayesian output
argumentation = medium
cohesion = low-medium
self-regulated revision = medium-high
lexical control = developing
6. Why this stage is useful
This stage makes the diagnosis richer.
Because after rules, you already know:
she is engaged
she revised
she needs stronger argument development
But after AI, you know more precisely:
what learner profile she fits
whether improvement is likely
which competencies are probably underdeveloped
That helps make the next stage —
final pedagogical decision
— more precise.
7. Very simple summary
This stage works like this:
clustering
tells you what type of student she is
Random Forest
tells you how likely she is to improve
Bayesian modelling
tells you what underlying skills she probably has
So the AI layer does
not give feedback directly
.
It gives
extra diagnostic intelligence
that helps you personalize the feedback better.

Stage 6:
Final pedagogical decision (you/system)
This is the stage where everything is brought together and turned into an
actual teaching action
. Up to this point, the system has collected data, classified indicators with thresholds, interpreted them through rules, and enriched the diagnosis through AI. Now the question becomes:
What feedback and onsite support should this particular student receive now?
So this stage is the
instructional decision point
.
1. What enters this stage
The final pedagogical decision uses the outputs from all previous stages:
From thresholds
You know which indicators are:
low
moderate
strong
From rules
You know the
pedagogical meaning
of those indicators:
weak argument development
partial feedback uptake
adaptive help-seeking
moderate cohesion
From AI
You know:
learner profile from clustering
predicted improvement from Random Forest
probable competence levels from Bayesian modelling
So this stage does
not
start from raw data.
It starts from a
full diagnostic profile
.
2. What happens here
At this stage, the system — under your pedagogical control — decides:
what kind of feedback to give
what to focus on
how explicit the feedback should be
what onsite intervention the teacher should deliver
So this is where the diagnosis becomes
personalized adaptive feedback
.
3. What the decision is based on
The decision combines three things:
A. The student’s main weakness
Example:
argument weak
cohesion developing
grammar acceptable
B. The student’s regulation pattern
Example:
revised once
asked for help
used resources
C. The student’s profile
Example:
engaged but developing writer
That combination determines the feedback.

, not only grammar correction.

Stage 7:
Student revises
This is the stage where the student
acts on the feedback
. It is very important because your study is not only about diagnosing problems, but about seeing whether the diagnosis and feedback actually lead to
change in the writing
.
So at this stage, the key question is:
Did the student use the feedback to improve the draft?
1. What happens here
After receiving the personalized feedback and onsite support, the student rewrites the paragraph.
This revised draft becomes
new evidence
.
Now you compare:
first draft
second draft
to see whether the targeted areas improved.
2. What you observe in the revision
You do not only check whether the student changed the text.
You check
what kind of change happened
.
Types of revision
surface revision
spelling, grammar, wording, punctuation
meaning-level revision
stronger explanation, better argument, clearer organization, improved evidence integration
This distinction is important because a student may revise a lot, but only correct surface errors.
3. What this stage means in your study
This stage tests whether the adaptive support worked.
If the feedback focused on:
argument development
cohesion
academic vocabulary
then the revised draft should show improvement in those same areas.
So this stage connects:
feedback focus → revision behaviour → improvement
4. For your student
For
Lahmarabbou Asmaa
, this stage already happened.
She received feedback saying:
the argument is present
explanation is limited
evidence should be stronger
reasoning should be expanded
structure should be revised
Then she submitted a second draft.
So in her case, Stage 7 means checking:
Did she clarify the argument more?
Did she improve explanation?
Did she organize the paragraph better?
Did she make vocabulary more academic?
5. What you found in her revision
From the comparison of Draft 1 and Draft 2:
She did revise
So this is
not
a non-response case.
She improved
Especially in:
clarity of the main idea
overall focus

But only partially
Because:
evidence integration still remained weak
reasoning was still not fully developed
cohesion improved only slightly
academic phrasing still needed work
So this stage shows:
the student
used the feedback
, but not fully.
That is called
partial feedback uptake
.
6. Why this stage matters theoretically
This stage is grounded in:
self-regulated learning
→ revision is part of monitoring and control
feedback literacy
→ students must not only receive feedback, but use it
writing process theory
→ writing improves through recursive drafting and revision
So revision is not just correction. It is the place where learning becomes visible.

You should document:
whether revision happened
how many revisions happened
whether revision was surface-level or meaning-level
which feedback points were actually addressed
which weaknesses remained

The revision stage provides the clearest evidence of whether adaptive feedback has been internalized and translated into writing improvement.

Stage 8:
Growth is measured across drafts
This is the final stage of the cycle. Here, you do not just ask whether the student revised. You ask:
Did the student actually improve, and in which areas?
So this stage measures
learning gain
.
1. What happens here
You compare the
first draft
and the
revised draft
using the same indicators:
rubric scores
text features
feedback focus
This allows you to see whether the student progressed in the exact areas that the adaptive feedback targeted.
2. What you measure
You measure growth in two main ways:
A. Product growth
Compare writing quality across drafts.
Examples:
argument score improved
cohesion improved
grammar improved
lexical resource improved
B. Text-analytics growth
Compare measurable textual indicators.
Examples:
word count increased or became more appropriate
cohesion markers increased
error density decreased
lexical diversity improved
3. Why this stage is so important
This stage is where your study proves that the system is not only diagnostic, but
effective
.
Without this step, you can only say:
the system identified problems
the system gave feedback
With this step, you can say:
the student improved after targeted feedback
That is what makes the intervention meaningful.
4. For your student
For
Lahmarabbou Asmaa
, growth across drafts can be described like this:
Growth observed
the paragraph became clearer
the argument was expressed more directly
the structure became more controlled
some wording improved
Limited growth
explanation remained only partly developed
evidence integration was still not fully strong
cohesion remained developing
academic lexical control remained uneven
So her growth pattern is:
real but partial improvement
That is a very important research finding.
5. What this means pedagogically
This tells you:
the feedback worked to some extent
the student is responsive to feedback
but she still needs continued support in higher-order writing
So the intervention should continue focusing on:
argument elaboration
cohesion
academic phrasing
6. What you can record
At this stage, you record things like:
Draft 1 score vs Draft 2 score
improvement in argumentation
improvement in organization/cohesion
remaining weaknesses
whether the change matched the feedback focus
This is where you validate your adaptive feedback system.
7. Simple explanation
In simple words:
This stage checks whether the student became a better writer after receiving personalized feedback and revising the draft.
For your student:
yes, she improved, but the improvement was partial rather than complete.
8. One sentence to remember
Growth analysis across drafts is the stage that confirms whether adaptive feedback has produced observable writing development.

Yes — the right solution for your study is
not
a fully autonomous AI. It should be a
human-in-the-loop assistant
that automates the repetitive analytics and drafting work, while
you keep control of thresholds, rules, rubric judgement, and final feedback approval
.
What the tool should do for you
The tool should automatically:
import Moodle logs and student drafts
compute process indicators and text indicators
compare them to your thresholds
trigger your rule-based interpretations
assign a learner profile
estimate likely performance and competence
draft personalized feedback and onsite intervention suggestions
generate a per-student report for you to review
You then do only four things:
check the diagnosis
correct anything pedagogically wrong
approve or edit the feedback

That is the safest design for workload
and

Run clustering
↓
Run Random Forest
↓
Run Bayesian inference
↓
Draft feedback + onsite intervention
↓
Show final report to teacher
↓
Teacher approves/edits
That means the tool does the heavy lifting, while you stay in the loop at the final decision point.
What the dashboard should show you
For each student, the dashboard should display:
student ID
task
draft number
process indicators
product indicators
threshold status: low / moderate / strong
triggered rules
learner profile
predicted score/gain
estimated competence levels
suggested personalized feedback
suggested onsite intervention
final teacher approval box
So instead of reading raw logs and essays one by one, you see a
ready-made diagnostic card
.
What you should NOT automate fully
To keep the study methodologically defensible, do
not
give the tool full control over:
final rubric scoring without your review
final pedagogical decisions without your approval
high-stakes feedback sent directly to students without checking
The tool should
draft
, not
replace
, your judgement.
That makes the study stronger because you can honestly say:
The system automated the analytical and diagnostic workload, while the instructor retained pedagogical oversight and final control.
The best way to build it with limited workload
Do this in
three phases
.
Phase 1: Minimum viable assistant
Build only:
data import
indicator extraction
thresholds
rules
feedback draft
dashboard
This alone will remove most of your workload.
Phase 2: Add learner profiling
Add:
clustering
profile labels
This improves adaptation.
Phase 3: Add advanced AI
Add:
Random Forest
Bayesian model
Only after the basic system is already working.
That order matters. If you try to build everything at once, the project will become too heavy.
What to give the engineer
You should give the engineer:
your task definitions
your rubric
your threshold table
your rule table
your feedback templates
sample Moodle exports
sample essay files
sample per-student output you want to see
So the engineer is not inventing pedagogy. They are implementing
your instructional logic
.
The cleanest division of labor
The tool does:
extraction
scoring support
profiling
prediction
draft feedback
report generation
You do:
threshold design
rule design
rubric supervision
final feedback approval
onsite teaching
My recommendation
Build
a Streamlit-based teacher assistant first
, with:
pandas
spaCy
scikit-learn
pgmpy
That stack is realistic, current, and well documented.
docs.streamlit.io

thresholds.yaml
YAML
thresholds:
word_count:
low: [0, 79]
moderate: [80, 119]
strong: [120, 1000]
time_on_task:
low: [0, 14.99]
moderate: [15, 29.99]
strong: [30, 300]
rubric_views:
low: [0, 0]
moderate: [1, 1]
strong: [2, 100]
revision_frequency:
low: [0, 0]
moderate: [1, 1]
strong: [2, 100]
error_density:
high_risk: [8.01, 100]
watch: [4, 8]
low_risk: [0, 3.99]
cohesion_index:
low: [0, 1]
moderate: [2, 3]
strong: [4, 100]
criterion_score:
low: [1, 2]
moderate: [3, 3]
strong: [4, 5]
feedback_uptake:
none: [0, 0]
partial: [1, 1]
strong: [2, 100]
help_seeking_messages:
none: [0, 0]
procedural: [1, 1]
adaptive: [2, 100]

3)
feedback_templates.yaml
YAML
feedback_templates:
criteria_referenced_prompt:
&gt;
Before revising, read the rubric carefully and compare your paragraph to the criteria.
Focus especially on organization, argumentation, and cohesion.
elaboration_prompt:
&gt;
Your paragraph is underdeveloped for this task.
Add one supporting idea and one explanatory sentence to make the argument clearer.
strategic_feedback:
&gt;
Your paragraph has a relevant idea, but its organization and cohesion need improvement.
Strengthen the topic sentence, improve transitions, and connect ideas more logically.
higher_order_feedback:
&gt;
Your paragraph needs stronger argument development.
Add clearer support, a relevant example, and explain how the evidence strengthens your claim.
direct_corrective_feedback:
&gt;
Your writing shows repeated grammatical difficulties.
Revise the highlighted errors and focus on the same grammar pattern across the paragraph.
lexical_enrichment_feedback:
&gt;
Your vocabulary is understandable, but too repetitive or informal for academic writing.
Replace basic words with more formal academic alternatives.
metacognitive_revision_prompt:
&gt;
Before you revise, identify the weakest part of your paragraph.
Decide what one change would improve the paragraph most.
feedforward_feedback:
&gt;
You viewed the feedback, but your revision is still limited.
Use the comments to make at least one content-level and one language-level improvement.
global_revision_feedback:
&gt;
You revised the text, but the main idea still needs stronger development.
Go beyond sentence correction and improve explanation, organization, and support.
dialogic_scaffolding:
&gt;
Your message shows active engagement.
We will discuss your difficulty together and revise one part of the paragraph collaboratively.
motivational_prompt:
&gt;
You need to engage more actively with the task materials.
Revisit the assignment, the model examples, and the rubric before writing again.
explicit_strategic_instruction:
&gt;
You are making effort, but your writing still needs structured support.
Focus on argument development, cohesion, and academic phrasing step by step.
4)
clustering.py
This groups students into learner profiles using
KMeans
. Scikit-learn’s
KMeans
takes an
n_clusters
argument, and the docs point to silhouette analysis as a way to choose that value;
fit_predict
is available for assigning cluster labels.
scikit-learn.org
+2
scikit-learn.org
+2
Python
Exécuter
import
pandas
as
pd
from
sklearn
.
preprocessing
import
StandardScaler
from
sklearn
.
cluster
import
KMeans
# Expected columns in df:
# student_id, time_on_task, revision_frequency, feedback_views,
# rubric_views, help_seeking_messages, total_score, ttr,
# cohesion_index, word_count
def
run_clustering
(
df
:
pd
.
DataFrame,
n_clusters
:
int
=
4
) -&gt;
pd
.
DataFrame:
features
=
[
"time_on_task"
,
"revision_frequency"
,
"feedback_views"
,
"rubric_views"
,
"help_seeking_messages"
,
"total_score"
,
"ttr"
,
"cohesion_index"
,
"word_count"
,
]
X
=
df
[
features
]
.
fillna(
0
)
scaler
=
StandardScaler
()
X_scaled
=
scaler
.
fit_transform(
X
)
model
=
KMeans
(
n_clusters
=
n_clusters
,
random_state
=
42
,
n_init
=
10
)
df
=
df
.
copy()
df
[
"cluster_label"
]
=
model
.
fit_predict(
X_scaled
)
centroids
=
pd
.
DataFrame(
scaler
.
inverse_transform(
model
.
cluster_centers_),
columns
=
features
)
centroids
[
"cluster_label"
]
=
range
(
n_clusters
)
return
df
,
centroids
5)
random_forest.py
This predicts total score or score gain. Scikit-learn’s
RandomForestRegressor
exposes
feature_importances_
, which is exactly what you need for reporting the strongest predictors.
scikit-learn.org

def
run_bayesian
(

:

.
DataFrame):

=

(

)

=

[

"revision_state"
,
"feedback_state"
,

,

,

,

,

),
(
"feedback_state"
,
"argument_state"
),
(
"time_state"
,

),

,

),
(

,

),
(

7) Minimal helper for applying rules
You didn’t explicitly ask for this file, but without it your thresholds and rules won’t execute. Paste this as
rule_engine.py
.
Python
Exécuter
import
operator
import
pandas
as
pd
import
yaml
OPS
=
{
"=="
:
operator
.
eq,
"&lt;="
:
operator
.
le,
"&gt;="
:
operator
.
ge,
"&lt;"
:
operator
.
lt,
"&gt;"
:
operator
.
gt,
}
def
parse_condition
(
expr
:
str
):
for
op
in
[
"&lt;="
,
"&gt;="
,
"=="
,
"&lt;"
,
"&gt;"
]:
if
expr
.
startswith(
op
):
return
op
,
float
(
expr
[
len
(
op
):])
raise
ValueError
(
f"Unsupported condition:
{
expr
}
"
)
def
matches_rule
(
row
:
pd
.
Series,
condition_dict
:
dict
) -&gt;
bool
:
for
field
,
expr
in
condition_dict
.
items():
op
,
value
=
parse_condition
(
expr
)
current
=
float
(
row
.
get(
field
,
0
))
if
not
OPS
[
op
](
current
,
value
):
return
False
return
True
def
apply_rules
(
df
:
pd
.
DataFrame,
rules_path
:
str
) -&gt;
pd
.
DataFrame:
with
open
(
rules_path
,
"r"
,
encoding
=
"utf-8"
)
as
f
:
rules_cfg
=
yaml
.
safe_load(
f
)[
"rules"
]
out
=
df
.
copy()
triggered_ids
=
[]
interpretations
=
[]
feedbacks
=
[]
interventions
=
[]
for
_
,
row
in
out
.
iterrows():
row_ids
=
[]
row_interpretations
=
[]
row_feedbacks
=
[]
row_interventions
=
[]
for
rule
in
rules_cfg
:
if
matches_rule
(
row
,
rule
[
"condition"
]):
row_ids
.
append(
rule
[
"rule_id"
])
row_interpretations
.
append(
rule
[
"interpretation"
])
row_feedbacks
.
append(
rule
[
"feedback_type"
])
row_interventions
.
append(
rule
[
"onsite_intervention"
])
triggered_ids
.
append(
"; "
.
join(
row_ids
))
interpretations
.
append(
"; "
.
join(
row_interpretations
))
feedbacks
.
append(
"; "
.
join(
row_feedbacks
))
interventions
.
append(
"; "
.
join(
row_interventions
))
out
[
"triggered_rule_ids"
]
=
triggered_ids
out
[
"interpretations"
]
=
interpretations
out
[
"feedback_types"
]
=
feedbacks
out
[
"onsite_interventions"
]
=
interventions
return
out
8) Minimal helper for generating feedback text

,

Yes — here is a
