Detailed Visual Diagram of the Study Architecture
┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    ADAPTIVE BLENDED ASSESSMENT THROUGH LEARNING ANALYTICS                   │
│                         TO ENHANCE EFL ACADEMIC WRITING DEVELOPMENT                         │
└──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
LAYER 1. THEORETICAL AND PEDAGOGICAL FOUNDATIONS
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
• Academic Writing Assessment (Weigle; Hyland)
• Self-Regulated Learning (Zimmerman)
• Learning Analytics (Siemens; Gašević)
• Feedback Literacy / Formative Feedback (Carless &amp; Boud; Hattie &amp; Timperley)
• Evidence-Centered Design / Probabilistic Inference (Mislevy; Murphy)
│
▼
LAYER 2. TASK DESIGN AND DATA GENERATION   [RESEARCHER-CONTROLLED]
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Writing task in Moodle:
• Argumentative essay / paragraph task
• Task instructions
• Rubric
• Model examples / resources
• Feedback channel
• Reflection activity
│
▼
LAYER 3. DATA COLLECTION
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
A. PROCESS DATA (Moodle traces)                   B. PRODUCT DATA (Writing output)
─────────────────────────────                    ─────────────────────────────────
• assignment views                              • draft 1 text
• rubric views                                  • draft 2 text
• resource access                               • rubric scores
• first access delay                            • word count
• time-on-task                                  • lexical diversity
• revision frequency                            • cohesion index
• feedback views                                • error density
• help-seeking messages                         • sentence length
• reflection entries                            • argument / organization quality
│
▼
LAYER 4. INDICATOR CONSTRUCTION
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Raw data are transformed into measurable indicators:
PROCESS INDICATORS                              PRODUCT INDICATORS
• engagement level                             • argumentation score
• planning behaviour                           • organization / cohesion score
• self-regulated revision                      • grammar accuracy
• feedback uptake                              • lexical resource
• help-seeking behaviour                       • academic style
• mechanics
• text-analytic features
│
▼
LAYER 5. THRESHOLD SPECIFICATION   [RESEARCHER-CONTROLLED]
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Each indicator is classified into pedagogical bands:
• Low / Risk
• Moderate / Developing
• Strong / Satisfactory
Examples:
• word count &lt; 80               → underdeveloped paragraph
• revision frequency = 0        → weak revision behaviour
• cohesion index &lt; 2            → weak discourse linking
• argument score ≤ 2            → weak reasoning
• feedback viewed + no revision → low feedback uptake
Threshold basis:
• task expectations
• analytic rubric logic
• writing theory
• SRL theory
• pilot calibration
│
▼
LAYER 6. RULE-BASED PEDAGOGICAL INTERPRETATION   [RESEARCHER-CONTROLLED]
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Thresholded indicators are translated into pedagogical meaning.
PRODUCT RULES
• low organization + low cohesion  → discourse organization problem
• low argumentation                → weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure
• high error density               → linguistic accuracy problem
• low lexical diversity            → restricted lexical repertoire
PROCESS RULES
• no revision                      → weak self-regulated revision
• feedback viewed, no revision     → weak feedback literacy
• low rubric consultation          → weak criteria awareness
• short time-on-task               → weak forethought regulation
• help-seeking after difficulty    → adaptive self-regulation
ENGAGEMENT RULES
• low views + low resource use     → disengagement
• high engagement + low quality    → effortful but struggling writer
• low engagement + acceptable text → efficient but fragile regulation
│
▼
LAYER 7. AI-SUPPORTED ANALYTICS
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
7A. LEARNER PROFILING (Clustering)
─────────────────────────────────
Input:
process + product + text indicators
Output:
learner profile / cluster label
Example clusters:
• strategic writer
• engaged but developing writer
• disengaged learner
• minimalist performer
│
▼
7B. PERFORMANCE PREDICTION (Random Forest)
──────────────────────────────────────────
Input:
behavioural + writing indicators
Output:
• predicted score / predicted improvement
• feature importance ranking
Purpose:
identify strongest predictors of writing growth
│
▼
7C. LATENT COMPETENCE INFERENCE (Bayesian Modelling)
────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Input:
observed evidence from process + product data
Latent competencies:
• argumentation competence
• cohesion competence
• linguistic / lexical control
• self-regulated revision
Output:
posterior probability of Low / Medium / High competence
│
▼
LAYER 8. DIAGNOSTIC SYNTHESIS
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
All outputs are combined into one learner diagnosis:
• threshold status
• triggered pedagogical rules
• learner profile
• predicted performance/growth
• inferred latent competence
Result:
A complete adaptive learner profile for each student
│
▼
LAYER 9. FINAL PEDAGOGICAL DECISION   [RESEARCHER + SYSTEM]
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
The system proposes, and the teacher supervises/validates:
• type of feedback needed
• degree of explicitness
• focus of feedback
• onsite instructional support
Possible feedback types:
• direct corrective feedback
• metalinguistic feedback
• strategic feedback
• metacognitive prompts
• feedforward guidance
• dialogic scaffolding
│
▼
LAYER 10. ONSITE ADAPTIVE INTERVENTION
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Teacher delivers targeted support, for example:
• rubric walkthrough
• paragraph restructuring support
• claim–evidence–explanation scaffold
• revision checklist
• vocabulary support
• clarification conference
• feedback decoding session
│
▼
LAYER 11. STUDENT REVISION CYCLE
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Student acts on feedback:
• revises draft
• resubmits text
• may seek clarification again
• engages in reflection
│
▼
LAYER 12. GROWTH MEASUREMENT ACROSS DRAFTS
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Improvement is evaluated by comparing Draft 1 and Draft 2:
• rubric score gain
• argumentation improvement
• cohesion improvement
• lexical development
• error reduction
• stronger revision quality
• better alignment with feedback focus
│
▼
LAYER 13. RESEARCH OUTPUTS / THESIS CONTRIBUTION
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
The study demonstrates whether:
• learning analytics can diagnose writing behaviour
• process indicators relate to product quality
• learner profiles can be identified
• writing performance can be predicted
• latent competencies can be inferred
• adaptive feedback improves academic writing development
│
▼
CONTINUOUS ADAPTIVE LOOP
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Writing Task → Data → Thresholds → Rules → AI Support → Pedagogical Decision
→ Feedback / Onsite Intervention → Revision → Growth → New Evidence

not just collecting Moodle data

. It is a
complete adaptive assessment cycle
in which:
students generate

,

thresholds and rules
,

,

students revise,

pedagogy-driven, analytics-informed, and AI-supported
.

Vous avez dit&nbsp;:
build a  strong rule table for the study :Exactly. The connection is:
raw data + AI outputs together drive the feedback decision.
More precisely:
Raw data -&gt; AI interprets the data -&gt; AI produces a learner-state output -&gt; the rule table maps that state to feedback.
So the AI does not replace the table.
It helps the table choose the right row.
Simple structure
Raw data: rubric views = 0, time-on-task = 8 min, word count = 60
AI output: “high probability of weak forethought/planning”
Rule table: if forethought weakness is high -&gt; give planning scaffold, rubric walkthrough, staged drafting support
Final output to learner: adaptive feedback message
So the relationship is:
Raw data = evidence
AI output = diagnosis
Rule table = pedagogical decision
Feedback shown to student = intervention
In one line
AI tells the system what the learner likely needs; the rule table tells the system what feedback to deliver for that need.
You can even rewrite the table conditions like this:
Forethought risk = high -&gt; planning support
Argumentation weakness = high -&gt; claim-evidence scaffold
Feedback uptake risk = high -&gt; feedback-decoding support
Revision depth = low -&gt; global revision prompt

doctoral-level, system-ready Rule Table
that integrates:
Raw data (indicators)
AI outputs (learner-state diagnosis)
Pedagogical interpretation
Adaptive feedback

Theoretical justification
This is the

.
🔷 MASTER RULE TABLE (AI-INFORMED ADAPTIVE PEDAGOGICAL ENGINE)
Structure Logic
Raw Data → Thresholds → AI Diagnosis → Rule Table → Feedback → Intervention
🔶 RULE TABLE
Rule ID
Raw Data Condition (Indicators)
AI Learner-State Output
Pedagogical Interpretation
Adaptive Feedback Type
Feedback Message Focus
Onsite Instructional Intervention
Theoretical Justification
🔹 1. FORETHOUGHT / PLANNING RULES
| A1 | rubric_views = 0 + time_on_task &lt; 15 + word_count &lt; 80 | High probability of weak forethought regulation | Student did not plan or consult criteria | Metacognitive + Feedforward | Plan before writing; use rubric; outline ideas | Rubric walkthrough + outline modelling + staged drafting | Zimmerman (2000); Nicol &amp; Macfarlane-Dick (2006) |
| A2 | delayed first access + short engagement | High procrastination risk | Weak goal-setting and task initiation | Planning scaffold | Break task into steps; set micro-goals | Timeline scaffolding + guided planning session | Zimmerman (2000) |
🔹 2. ARGUMENTATION RULES
| B1 | argument_score ≤ 2 + cohesion low | Low argument competence probability | Weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure | Strategic (higher-order) | Add example + explain link to claim | Claim–evidence–explanation modelling | Hyland (2019); Weigle (2002) |
| B2 | argument_score = 3 + revision present | Medium argument competence | Partial development but insufficient depth | Strategic + Metacognitive | Expand reasoning; justify claims more clearly | Guided expansion of paragraph reasoning | Flower &amp; Hayes (1981) |
🔹 3. LINGUISTIC ACCURACY RULES
| B3 | error_density &gt; 8 + grammar_score ≤ 2 | High probability of linguistic difficulty | Persistent grammar errors affecting clarity | Direct corrective feedback | Correct repeated errors; focus on pattern | Mini grammar lesson + focused practice | Ferris; Weigle (2002) |
| B4 | moderate errors + revision attempts | Medium linguistic control | Errors persist despite effort | Metalinguistic feedback | Explain rule; guide self-correction | Guided correction activity | Vygotsky (ZPD) |
🔹 4. LEXICAL DEVELOPMENT RULES
| B5 | low TTR + lexical_score ≤ 2 | Low lexical sophistication | Limited vocabulary range | Lexical enrichment feedback | Replace basic words with academic vocabulary | Academic vocabulary modelling | Hyland (2005) |
| B6 | help-seeking about vocabulary | Adaptive lexical awareness | Student actively regulating language use | Dialogic feedback | Confirm and refine lexical choices | Vocabulary clarification discussion | Zimmerman (2000) |
🔹 5. REVISION &amp; SRL RULES
| C1 | revision_frequency = 0 | Weak self-regulated revision | Product-oriented writing | Metacognitive prompt | Revise structure and ideas, not only grammar | Revision checklist + guided rewriting | Hayes (2012); Zimmerman (2000) |
| C2 | revision ≥ 1 + no improvement in argument | Surface-level revision detected | Student edits form but not meaning | Strategic revision feedback | Improve explanation, not just wording | Teacher modelling of global revision | Flower &amp; Hayes (1981) |
🔹 6. FEEDBACK LITERACY RULES
| C3 | feedback viewed + no revision | Low feedback uptake probability | Feedback not internalized | Feedforward feedback | Show how to apply feedback | Feedback decoding session | Carless &amp; Boud (2018) |
| C4 | feedback viewed + partial revision | Medium feedback literacy | Partial understanding of feedback | Guided feedforward | Improve deeper aspects (argument, cohesion) | Example-based revision discussion | Hattie &amp; Timperley (2007) |
🔹 7. ENGAGEMENT RULES
| D1 | low views + low access + no submission | High disengagement risk | Student not participating | Motivational + simplified task support | Re-engage with task step-by-step | Teacher check-in + simplified scaffold | Siemens (2013) |
| D2 | high engagement + low product score | Effortful but struggling learner | Student works but lacks strategy | Explicit strategic instruction | Focus on structure and argument | Scaffolded writing support session | Dawson (2018) |
| D3 | low engagement + acceptable performance | Efficient but unstable learner | Performance without stable process | Metacognitive prompting | Encourage reflection and planning | Light monitoring + reflection tasks | Zimmerman (2000) |
🔹 8. HELP-SEEKING RULES (VERY IMPORTANT FOR YOUR STUDY)
| D4 | help-seeking message (conceptual/linguistic) | High adaptive self-regulation probability | Student actively monitors difficulty | Dialogic scaffolding | Clarify misunderstanding and guide solution | One-to-one micro-conference | Zimmerman (2000); SRL |
| D5 | no help-seeking + weak performance | Passive regulation / hidden difficulty | Student not recognizing problems | Metacognitive + motivational prompt | Encourage asking for help | Proactive teacher intervention | Siemens (2013) |

Full pipeline:

↓
Threshold Classification
↓
AI Output (probabilities, clusters, predictions)
↓
Rule Table selects matching pedagogical condition
↓
Feedback type + message generated
↓
Teacher validates
↓
Student receives adaptive feedback

🔷 CORE LOGIC OF THE SYSTEM
RAW DATA (evidence)
↓
THRESHOLDS (categorisation)
↓
AI OUTPUT (learner-state diagnosis)
↓
RULE TABLE (pedagogical mapping)

🔷 MASTER AI-INFORMED RULE TABLE
Structure
| Rule ID | Raw Data Indicators (Thresholded) | AI Diagnosis (Learner State) | Pedagogical Interpretation | Feedback Strategy | Adaptive Feedback (What student receives) | Onsite Intervention | Theoretical Grounding |
🔶 1. FORETHOUGHT &amp; PLANNING (SRL – Forethought Phase)
| A1 | rubric_views = LOW + time_on_task = LOW + word_count = LOW | Forethought risk = HIGH | Lack of planning and criteria awareness | Metacognitive + Feedforward | “Before writing, review the rubric and create a short outline. Define your main claim and one supporting idea.” | Rubric walkthrough + outline modelling | Zimmerman (2000); Nicol &amp; Macfarlane-Dick (2006) |
| A2 | first_access_delay = HIGH + late start | Procrastination risk = HIGH | Weak goal-setting and task initiation | Planning scaffold | “Break the task into steps: idea → example → explanation. Start with a simple plan before writing.” | Timeline scaffolding | Zimmerman (2000) |
🔶 2. ARGUMENTATION (Cognitive Writing Quality)
| B1 | argument_score = LOW | Argumentation weakness = HIGH | Weak claim–evidence–reasoning structure | Strategic feedback | “Your paragraph needs stronger support. Add a clear example and explain how it supports your main idea.” | Claim–evidence–explanation modelling | Hyland (2019); Weigle (2002) |
| B2 | argument_score = MODERATE + revision present | Argumentation = MEDIUM | Partial development | Strategic + Metacognitive | “Your idea is clear, but your explanation needs expansion. Why does your example support your claim?” | Guided reasoning expansion | Flower &amp; Hayes (1981) |
🔶 3. COHESION &amp; ORGANIZATION
| B3 | organization = LOW + cohesion_index = LOW | Discourse competence = LOW | Weak paragraph structure and transitions | Strategic feedback | “Improve the organization of your paragraph. Use connectors (e.g., however, therefore) and link your ideas clearly.” | Paragraph restructuring with model | Halliday &amp; Hasan; Hyland (2019) |
🔶 4. LINGUISTIC ACCURACY
| B4 | error_density = HIGH + grammar_score = LOW | Linguistic difficulty = HIGH | Persistent grammar errors | Direct corrective feedback | “Focus on correcting repeated grammar errors (e.g., verb tense, agreement). Revise the same pattern throughout your paragraph.” | Mini grammar lesson | Ferris; Weigle (2002) |
| B5 | error_density = MODERATE + revision present | Linguistic control = MEDIUM | Partial control, needs guidance | Metalinguistic feedback | “Check this grammar pattern. Why is this form incorrect? Try correcting it yourself.” | Guided correction | Vygotsky (ZPD) |
🔶 5. LEXICAL DEVELOPMENT
| B6 | lexical_score = LOW + TTR = LOW | Lexical competence = LOW | Limited vocabulary range | Lexical enrichment | “Replace basic words with more academic vocabulary (e.g., ‘big problem’ → ‘significant issue’).” | Vocabulary modelling | Hyland (2005) |
| B7 | help-seeking (lexical) | Adaptive lexical awareness | Active vocabulary regulation | Dialogic feedback | “Your word choice is acceptable; here is a more formal alternative.” | Micro-conference | Zimmerman (2000) |
🔶 6. REVISION &amp; SELF-REGULATION
| C1 | revision_frequency = 0 | Revision depth = LOW | No monitoring or control | Metacognitive prompt | “Revise your paragraph: improve one idea, one example, and one sentence structure.” | Revision checklist | Hayes (2012); Zimmerman (2000) |
| C2 | revision ≥ 1 + no improvement | Surface revision | Editing without meaning change | Strategic revision feedback | “Focus on improving your argument, not only correcting grammar.” | Global revision modelling | Flower &amp; Hayes (1981) |
🔶 7. FEEDBACK LITERACY
| C3 | feedback_viewed = YES + no revision | Feedback uptake = LOW | Feedback not internalized | Feedforward | “Use the feedback to improve your argument and organization. Revise one part based on the comment.” | Feedback decoding session | Carless &amp; Boud (2018) |
| C4 | feedback_viewed + partial revision | Feedback uptake = MEDIUM | Partial understanding | Guided feedforward | “You improved some parts, now focus on deeper explanation.” | Example-based revision | Hattie &amp; Timperley (2007) |
🔶 8. ENGAGEMENT (Learning Analytics)
| D1 | low views + low resource access | Engagement risk = HIGH | Disengagement | Motivational + scaffold | “Revisit the assignment instructions and examples before writing again.” | Teacher check-in | Siemens (2013) |
| D2 | high engagement + low score | Effortful struggling learner | Effort without strategy | Explicit instruction | “Focus on structure and argument, not only effort.” | Scaffolded support session | Dawson (2018) |
| D3 | low engagement + acceptable score | Fragile regulation | Unstable learning strategy | Metacognitive prompt | “Reflect on your writing process. How did you plan your paragraph?” | Light monitoring | Zimmerman (2000) |
🔶 9. HELP-SEEKING (KEY CONTRIBUTION OF YOUR STUDY)
| D4 | help-seeking message (conceptual/linguistic) | Adaptive SRL = HIGH | Active monitoring and regulation | Dialogic scaffolding | “Let’s clarify your difficulty and improve this sentence together.” | One-to-one micro feedback | Zimmerman (2000) |
| D5 | weak performance + no help-seeking | Passive learner | Hidden difficulty | Motivational + metacognitive | “If you are unsure, ask for clarification. Try to identify your difficulty.” | Proactive teacher intervention | Siemens (2013) |

| Forethought risk = HIGH | A1 | Weak planning and criteria awareness | planning_scaffold | “Before writing, review the rubric and create a short outline with your main idea and one supporting point.” | Rubric walkthrough + outline modelling | Zimmerman (2000); Nicol &amp; Macfarlane-Dick (2006) |

🔶 2. ARGUMENTATION
| Argumentation = LOW | B1 | Weak claim–evidence–reasoning | argument_scaffold | “Add a clear example and explain how it supports your main idea.” | Claim–evidence–explanation modelling | Hyland (2019); Weigle (2002) |
| Argumentation = MEDIUM | B2 | Underdeveloped reasoning | argument_expansion | “Your idea is clear, but explain it more. Why does your example support your claim?” | Guided expansion | Flower &amp; Hayes (1981) |
| Argumentation = HIGH | B3 | Strong reasoning | metacognitive_extension | “Your argument is strong. Try adding a counter-argument to deepen your analysis.” | Advanced writing challenge | Hyland (2019) |
🔶 3. COHESION &amp; ORGANIZATION
| Discourse competence = LOW | B4 | Weak organization and transitions | cohesion_support | “Use connectors (however, therefore) to link your ideas and improve flow.” | Paragraph restructuring | Halliday &amp; Hasan; Hyland (2019) |
| Discourse competence = MEDIUM | B5 | Partial coherence | organization_support | “Your paragraph is organized, but transitions can be improved.” | Model paragraph comparison | Hyland (2019) |
🔶 4. LINGUISTIC ACCURACY
| Linguistic control = LOW | B6 | Frequent grammar errors | direct_corrective_feedback | “Revise repeated grammar errors (tense, agreement) across your paragraph.” | Mini grammar lesson | Ferris; Weigle (2002) |
| Linguistic control = MEDIUM | B7 | Partial control | metalinguistic_feedback | “Check this grammar pattern and try correcting it yourself.” | Guided correction | Vygotsky |
| Linguistic control = HIGH | B8 | Strong accuracy | fluency_extension | “Your language is accurate. Focus now on improving style and variation.” | Advanced refinement | Hyland |
🔶 5. LEXICAL DEVELOPMENT
| Lexical competence = LOW | B9 | Limited vocabulary | lexical_enrichment | “Replace simple words with more academic vocabulary.” | Vocabulary modelling | Hyland (2005) |
| Lexical competence = MEDIUM | B10 | Developing vocabulary | lexical_refinement | “Try to use more precise and formal expressions.” | Synonym training | Hyland |
🔶 6. REVISION (SRL PERFORMANCE PHASE)
| Revision depth = LOW | C1 | No or superficial revision | revision_prompt | “Revise your paragraph: improve one idea, one example, and one sentence.” | Revision checklist | Hayes (2012); Zimmerman (2000) |
| Revision depth = MEDIUM | C2 | Partial revision | global_revision_feedback | “You revised your text, but improve explanation and organization further.” | Teacher modelling | Flower &amp; Hayes |
| Revision depth = HIGH | C3 | Strong revision behaviour | metacognitive_feedback | “Your revision is effective. Reflect on what strategy helped you most.” | Reflection task | Zimmerman |
🔶 7. FEEDBACK UPTAKE
| Feedback uptake = LOW | C4 | Feedback ignored | feedback_decoding | “Use the feedback comments to improve your paragraph before resubmitting.” | Feedback interpretation session | Carless &amp; Boud (2018) |

🔶 8. ENGAGEMENT

🔶 9. HELP-SEEKING (YOUR UNIQUE CONTRIBUTION)

| Predicted improvement = HIGH | E2 | High growth potential | targeted_development_feedback | “You are improving. Focus now on refining specific weaknesses.” | Targeted support | EDM |

Example of how the rule table works
Step 1. Raw data collected

Forethought risk = High

the student did not plan well
the paragraph is underdeveloped
the argument is weak
the student saw feedback but did not revise

Before rewriting, read the rubric carefully and prepare a short outline with your main claim and one supporting idea.

Yes — I’ll show you
exactly how the AI outputs are produced
from each method.
Important first point:
Clustering
gives a
profile label
Random Forest
gives a
prediction
Bayesian modelling
gives a
probability distribution
So the AI outputs are
not invented
. They come from different calculations.
1. Clustering → how the AI gets the learner profile
What goes in
The model takes a set of features for each student, for example:
assignment views
resource access
rubric views
time-on-task
revision frequency
feedback views
help-seeking messages
word count
cohesion index
total score
So for one student, the system creates a row like this:
Variable
Asmaa example
assignment views
high
resource access
4
rubric views
1
time-on-task
moderate
revision frequency
1
feedback views
present
help-seeking
1

198
cohesion index
moderate
total score
moderate
In real modelling, these are numeric.
What the model does
K-means clustering works like this:
Step A
It standardizes all variables so they are on the same scale.
Step B
It compares the student to the
cluster centers
.
Example cluster centers after training on all students:
Cluster
General meaning
C1
strategic writers
C2
engaged but developing writers
C3
disengaged / low-performance learners
Step C
It calculates
distance
from Asmaa to each cluster center.
Illustrative example:
distance to C1 = 2.8
distance to C2 = 1.1
distance to C3 = 3.5
The
smallest distance wins
.
So:
Asmaa → Cluster 2
AI output from clustering
The AI output is:
Learner profile = Engaged but developing writer
That is how the profile is produced.
So clustering output =
nearest learner type
.
2. Random Forest → how the AI gets the predicted performance
What goes in
Random Forest uses the same kind of features, but now it has a
target
to predict.
Example target:
final score
or
score gain between draft 1 and draft 2
For your study, a good target is:
score_gain = draft2 score − draft1 score
What the model does
A Random Forest is a large set of decision trees.
Each tree asks questions like:
Is revision frequency &gt; 0?
Is cohesion index &gt; 2?
Is help-seeking present?
Is word count above 120?
Is argument score already moderate?
Each tree gives a prediction.
Example with 5 trees only
Imagine 5 trees predict Asmaa’s score gain like this:
Tree 1 → +5
Tree 2 → +6
Tree 3 → +4
Tree 4 → +5
Tree 5 → +6
The forest averages them:
Predicted gain = (5 + 6 + 4 + 5 + 6) / 5 = 5.2
If the full model had 200 trees, it would average across all 200.
AI output from Random Forest
The AI output is something like:
Predicted improvement = Moderate
Predicted score gain = +5.2
It also gives
feature importance
.
Example:
Variable
Importance
revision frequency
0.28
cohesion index
0.21
feedback views
0.17
word count
0.12
help-seeking
0.10
This means the model learned that
revision frequency
was the strongest predictor of improvement.
So Random Forest output =
predicted result + strongest predictors
.
3. Bayesian modelling → how the AI gets competence probabilities
What goes in
Bayesian modelling uses:
prior probabilities
observed evidence
Example latent competence
Argumentation competence:
Low
Medium
High
Example observed evidence
For Asmaa:
argument score = moderate
revision frequency = 1
help-seeking = yes
cohesion = moderate
word count = 198
What the model does
Bayesian modelling starts with a
prior belief
.
Example prior for argumentation competence:
Level
Prior probability
Low
0.30
Medium
0.50
High
0.20
Then it updates this prior using the evidence.
Example evidence logic
If revision is present, probability of medium/high competence rises
If help-seeking is adaptive, self-regulation probability rises
If argument score is moderate, “low” drops a bit and “medium” rises
If cohesion is moderate, discourse competence remains developing
After updating, the model may produce this posterior:
Argumentation competence
Posterior probability
Low
0.18
Medium
0.57
High
0.25
So the model concludes:
Argumentation competence = most likely Medium
For self-regulated revision it may produce:
SRL revision competence
Posterior probability
Low
0.12
Medium
0.43
High
0.45
So the output becomes:
Self-regulated revision = Medium–High
AI output from Bayesian modelling
The AI output is:
Argument competence: Medium (0.57)
Cohesion competence: Medium (0.50)
Self-regulated revision: Medium–High (0.45 high, 0.43 medium)
Lexical competence: Developing
So Bayesian output =
probabilities of hidden competence states
.
4. Put all three together for your student
For Asmaa, the AI outputs would look like this:
Method
Output type

Bayesian

5. Why these outputs are different
Because each model answers a different question:
Clustering
Who is this student similar to?
Random Forest
What outcome is likely?
Bayesian
What hidden competence level is most probable?
That is why you need all three outputs separated.
6. How these outputs enter the rule table
Once these outputs exist, the rule table reads them like this:
AI output
Rule decision
engaged but developing writer
give strategic support, not motivation
predicted moderate improvement
support weakest area to increase gain
argument competence = medium
choose argument expansion feedback
cohesion competence = medium
choose cohesion support feedback
SRL revision = medium-high
do not treat as disengaged
So:
AI output → rule selection → feedback template
7. Final simplified explanation
You can think of it like this:
Clustering
says: “This student belongs to this learner type.”
Random Forest
says: “This student is likely to improve by this amount.”
Bayesian modelling
says: “This student probably has these competence levels.”
Those three outputs are then used by the system to choose the correct feedback.
8. One very clear sentence for your thesis
Clustering generated learner-profile labels, Random Forest generated predicted performance outcomes and feature importance, and Bayesian modelling generated posterior probabilities of latent writing competencies; together, these AI outputs informed the rule-based selection of adaptive feedback.

Here is the
feedback template library
linked to the template labels from the previous table.
Feedback Template Library
Feedback Template Label
Purpose
Student-Facing Feedback Text
planning_scaffold
Support weak forethought and criteria awareness
Before rewriting, read the rubric carefully and make a short outline. Write your main claim, one supporting point, and one explanation before you begin drafting.
planning_prompt
Strengthen partial planning
Your paragraph would improve with clearer planning. Before writing, note your topic sentence, one example, and the explanation you want to add.
motivational_reengagement
Re-engage disengaged learners
Revisit the assignment instructions, rubric, and model examples before writing again. Focus on one clear step at a time so you can complete the task with confidence.
explicit_strategic_instruction
Support effortful but struggling learners
You are making a clear effort, but your paragraph needs stronger structure and support. Focus on organizing your ideas and explaining how your evidence supports your argument.
metacognitive_prompt
Encourage self-monitoring and reflection
Before revising, ask yourself: What is the main weakness in my paragraph? What one change would improve it the most? Revise with that goal in mind.
argument_scaffold
Address major weakness in claim–evidence–reasoning
Your paragraph needs stronger argument development. Add one clear example and explain directly how it supports your main claim.
argument_expansion
Develop partially formed argumentation
Your main idea is relevant, but it needs deeper explanation. After giving your example, explain why it proves your point.
metacognitive_extension
Extend strong writers
Your argument is already strong. To make it more advanced, add a counter-argument or deepen your explanation of the issue.
cohesion_support
Improve weak transitions and flow
Your ideas are relevant, but the paragraph needs better flow. Use linking words such as however, therefore, furthermore, or in addition to connect your ideas more clearly.
organization_support
Improve sequencing and paragraph unity
Your paragraph is generally organized, but the order of ideas can be clearer. Make sure each sentence supports the main point and follows a logical sequence.
direct_corrective_feedback
Address repeated grammar problems
Your writing contains repeated grammar problems that reduce clarity. Revise the highlighted errors and correct the same pattern throughout the paragraph.
metalinguistic_feedback
Support guided grammar self-correction
Check the grammar pattern in the highlighted sentence. Think about the rule, then try correcting the sentence yourself before resubmitting.
fluency_extension
Extend accurate writers stylistically
Your language is accurate. Now focus on making your paragraph more fluent and academic by varying sentence structure and using more precise wording.
lexical_enrichment
Improve limited vocabulary range
Your vocabulary is understandable, but it is too basic or repetitive for academic writing. Replace common words with more formal and precise academic alternatives.
lexical_refinement
Improve partially developed lexical control
Your vocabulary is developing well, but some expressions can be more precise. Revise a few key words so your paragraph sounds more academic.
revision_prompt
Prompt learners to revise meaningfully
Revise your paragraph by improving one idea, one example, and one sentence pattern. Do not focus only on grammar; improve the meaning and support as well.
global_revision_feedback
Move revision beyond surface editing
You revised your paragraph, but you still need to improve explanation, organization, and support. Go beyond sentence correction and strengthen the whole paragraph.
metacognitive_feedback
Reinforce effective revision behaviour
Your revision shows real improvement. Reflect on what strategy helped you most so you can use it again in your next paragraph.
feedback_decoding
Support weak feedback uptake
You viewed the feedback, but your draft does not yet show enough change. Re-read the comments and apply at least one improvement to your ideas and one to your language.
feedforward_guidance
Guide partial feedback uptake
You improved some parts of the paragraph. Now use the feedback to deepen your explanation and improve the structure of your ideas.
reinforcement_feedback
Reinforce strong feedback use
You used the feedback effectively. Continue refining your paragraph by strengthening your argument and making your writing more precise.
dialogic_scaffolding
Support adaptive help-seeking
Your question shows good engagement with the writing task. We will clarify the problem together and improve that part of the paragraph step by step.
intensive_support_plan
Provide strong intervention for low predicted improvement
You need more structured support to improve this paragraph. Focus first on your weakest area, then revise step by step with guidance.
targeted_development_feedback
Focus support when growth potential is good
You are showing potential for improvement. Focus now on the one area that most limits your paragraph so your next revision becomes stronger.
argument_refinement
Support medium Bayesian argument competence
Your argument is developing, but it still needs clearer support and fuller explanation. Strengthen the link between your evidence and your claim.
metacognitive_training
Support weak self-regulated revision competence
Try to manage your writing process more actively: plan before writing, check your progress while drafting, and evaluate your paragraph after revising.

Vous avez dit&nbsp;:
briefly what is the role of ai in this study , answer professionally
ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
In this study, the role of AI is to function as a
diagnostic and decision-support component
within the adaptive assessment system. It does not replace the teacher or the pedagogical framework; rather, it processes behavioural, textual, and performance data in order to identify learner profiles, predict writing development, and estimate underlying writing competencies. More specifically, clustering is used to profile students according to engagement and performance patterns, Random Forest models are used to predict writing performance and identify the most important factors influencing improvement, and Bayesian modelling is used to estimate latent competencies such as argumentation, cohesion, and self-regulated revision. These AI-generated outputs then inform the rule-based pedagogical layer, which maps learner states onto targeted feedback and onsite instructional intervention. Thus, AI serves as an
analytics-driven support mechanism
that enhances the precision, personalization, and responsiveness of academic writing assessment.
Vous avez dit&nbsp;:
now act as an AI developper and  start from the begining and show me exactly how to program this system  starting from how to cluster profiles , random forest , bayesian to rules , thresholds , ai outputs then selected feedback
ChatGPT a dit&nbsp;:
I’m going to lay this out as a build sequence: data model first, then thresholds and rules, then clustering, Random Forest, Bayesian inference, and finally feedback selection. After that I’ll give you a minimal but working code skeleton you can hand to an engineer.
Thought for 41s
Below is the cleanest way to
program the system from the beginning
.
The build order should be:
merge the data
compute indicators
apply thresholds
apply rules
run clustering
run Random Forest
run Bayesian inference
map AI outputs to feedback templates
show the result to you for approval
That order matters.
pandas.merge
is appropriate for combining your tables,
KMeans
is the standard scikit-learn clustering model,
RandomForestRegressor
is the correct forest model for predicting scores or score gains, and
pgmpy
’s
DiscreteBayesianNetwork
with
VariableElimination
is a suitable way to implement the competence-inference layer.
pgmpy

1. Project structure
Create this folder structure:
adaptive_writing_system/
├── data/
│   ├── moodle_logs.csv
│   ├── rubric_scores.csv
│   ├── essays.csv
│   └── messages.csv
├── config/
│   ├── thresholds.yaml
│   ├── rules.yaml
│   └── feedback_templates.yaml
├── outputs/
├── app/
│   ├── utils.py
│   ├── merge_data.py
│   ├── text_features.py
│   ├── threshold_engine.py
│   ├── rule_engine.py
│   ├── clustering_engine.py
│   ├── random_forest_engine.py
│   ├── bayesian_engine.py
│   ├── feedback_engine.py
│   └── run_pipeline.py
└── requirements.txt
2. Install dependencies
Bash
pip install pandas pyyaml scikit-learn pgmpy
3. Define the input data format
Your system needs four input files.
moodle_logs.csv
One row per student-task-draft.
csv
student_id,task_id,draft_no,assignment_views,rubric_views,resource_access_count,first_access_delay_minutes,time_on_task,revision_frequency,feedback_views
S01,T1,1,6,1,3,12,24,0,1
S01,T1,2,8,2,4,5,35,1,2
rubric_scores.csv
csv
student_id,task_id,draft_no,task_achievement,organization,argumentation,cohesion,lexical_resource,grammar_accuracy,academic_style,mechanics,total_score
S01,T1,1,3,2,2,2,2,3,2,3,19
S01,T1,2,4,3,3,3,3,3,3,3,25
essays.csv
csv
student_id,task_id,draft_no,essay_text
S01,T1,1,"Another major worry is how AI impacts fairness and student relationships..."
S01,T1,2,"Another big worry about intelligence in education is how it affects fairness..."
messages.csv
csv
student_id,task_id,draft_no,help_seeking_messages,message_type,message_text
S01,T1,2,1,language_help,"I am not sure if the vocabulary sounds formal enough..."
4. Create the configuration files
config/thresholds.yaml
YAML
thresholds:
word_count:
low: [0, 79]
moderate: [80, 119]
strong: [120, 1000]
time_on_task:
low: [0, 14.99]
moderate: [15, 29.99]
strong: [30, 1000]
rubric_views:
low: [0, 0]
moderate: [1, 1]
strong: [2, 100]
revision_frequency:
low: [0, 0]
moderate: [1, 1]
strong: [2, 100]
error_density:
high_risk: [8.01, 100]
watch: [4, 8]
low_risk: [0, 3.99]
cohesion_index:
low: [0, 1]
moderate: [2, 3]
strong: [4, 100]
criterion_score:
low: [1, 2]
moderate: [3, 3]
strong: [4, 5]
config/rules.yaml
YAML
rules:
- rule_id: A1
condition:
rubric_views:
"==0"
first_access_delay_minutes:
"&gt;30"
ai_state_required:
"Forethought risk = High"
interpretation:
"Weak forethought regulation and low criteria awareness"
feedback_template:
"planning_scaffold"
onsite_intervention:
"rubric_walkthrough_and_outline_support"
- rule_id: B1
condition:
argumentation:
"&lt;=2"
ai_state_required:
"Argumentation weakness = High"
interpretation:
"Weak claim-evidence-reasoning structure"
feedback_template:
"argument_scaffold"
onsite_intervention:
"claim_evidence_explanation_modelling"
- rule_id: B4
condition:
organization:
"&lt;=2"
cohesion_index:
"&lt;2"
ai_state_required:
"Discourse competence = Low"
interpretation:
"Weak organization and cohesion"
feedback_template:
"cohesion_support"
onsite_intervention:
"paragraph_restructuring_with_model"
- rule_id: C1
condition:
revision_frequency:
"==0"
ai_state_required:
"Revision depth = Low"
interpretation:
"Weak self-regulated revision"
feedback_template:
"revision_prompt"
onsite_intervention:
"guided_revision_checklist"
- rule_id: C4
condition:
feedback_views:
"&gt;=1"
revision_frequency:
"==0"
ai_state_required:
"Feedback uptake risk = High"
interpretation:
"Feedback viewed but not used"
feedback_template:
"feedback_decoding"
onsite_intervention:
"feedback_interpretation_session"
- rule_id: D4
condition:
help_seeking_messages:
"&gt;=1"
ai_state_required:
"Help-seeking regulation = Adaptive"
interpretation:
"Active clarification-seeking"
feedback_template:
"dialogic_scaffolding"
onsite_intervention:
"short_clarification_conference"
config/feedback_templates.yaml
YAML
feedback_templates:
planning_scaffold:
&gt;
Before rewriting, read the rubric carefully and create a short outline
with your main idea, one supporting point, and one explanation.
argument_scaffold:
&gt;
Your paragraph needs stronger argument development. Add one relevant example
and explain clearly how it supports your main claim.
cohesion_support:
&gt;
Improve the flow of your paragraph by using transitions and linking each
sentence more clearly to the main idea.
revision_prompt:
&gt;
Revise your paragraph by improving one idea, one example, and one sentence.
Do not focus only on grammar; improve the meaning as well.
feedback_decoding:
&gt;
You viewed the feedback, but your draft does not yet show enough change.
Re-read the comments and apply at least one content-level improvement.
dialogic_scaffolding:
&gt;
Your question shows good engagement. We will clarify the difficulty together
and improve that part of the paragraph step by step.

8. Produce AI outputs
This happens in three separate engines.

KMeans groups similar learners. Scikit-learn’s implementation supports choosing

, initializing with

.

8B. Random Forest
A random forest regressor fits many trees on subsamples and averages predictions; this is why it is useful for stable score/gain prediction.
scikit-learn.org

Yes. I will give you the
full sequence of your study
as one coherent research architecture, and I will explain
why each step leads to the next one
so you can clearly see the internal logic of the thesis.
Your study should not be presented as separate parts like writing assessment, learning analytics, AI, and feedback. It should be presented as
one continuous adaptive cycle
in which each stage produces the evidence needed for the next stage.
Complete sequence of the study
1. Define the writing task and assessment framework
You begin by designing the instructional context of the study: the academic writing task, the rubric, the Moodle environment, the learning resources, and the feedback channels.
This step is essential because the whole study depends on
what kind of writing performance you want to observe and improve
. If the task is not clearly defined, then the data, the analytics, and the feedback will all lack validity.
This stage gives you:
the writing task students complete
the rubric used to assess performance
the process environment where behavioural data are generated
Why this step must come first
Because every later stage depends on it:
process data only make sense in relation to a task
product data only make sense in relation to an assessment framework
adaptive feedback only makes sense if the expected performance is clearly defined
So this step establishes the
pedagogical and methodological foundation
of the study.
2. Generate learner data through writing in Moodle
Students then complete the writing task in Moodle. While doing so, they produce two types of evidence:
Process data
How they work:
assignment views
rubric consultation
resource access
time-on-task
revision behaviour
feedback views
help-seeking messages
Product data
What they write:
the paragraph or essay itself
rubric scores
textual features such as word count, cohesion, lexical diversity, and error density
This step is important because your study is built on the idea that academic writing development must be understood through
both process and product
, not product alone.
Why this step follows task design
Because students must first interact with the task before any valid evidence can exist.
Why this step matters
Because it creates the raw evidence base of the study. Without this stage, nothing can be analysed.
3. Construct indicators from the raw data
At this stage, raw logs and raw texts are transformed into meaningful indicators.
For example:
From process data:
number of assignment views
revision frequency
feedback uptake
help-seeking frequency
From product data:
argumentation score
cohesion score
lexical diversity
error density
This step is necessary because raw data alone are not analytically useful. A log line or a paragraph by itself does not yet tell you what you need to know. You must convert it into
measurable variables
.
Why this step follows data generation
Because you must first collect the evidence before you can transform it into analyzable indicators.
Why this step matters
Because it creates the actual variables that power the entire analytical system.
4. Apply thresholds to classify the indicators
Once indicators are available, you classify them into levels such as:
low / risk
moderate / developing
strong / satisfactory
For example:
word count below 80 may indicate underdevelopment
revision frequency of zero may indicate weak self-regulated revision
argument score of 1–2 may indicate weak reasoning
This step is important because indicators are still just values until they are interpreted against a meaningful standard.
Why this step follows indicator construction
Because you must first know the value of each indicator before you can judge whether it is weak, moderate, or strong.
Why this step matters
Because thresholds create the first diagnostic signal. They transform numbers into
pedagogically meaningful categories
.
5. Interpret the thresholded indicators through rule-based pedagogy
After thresholding, the system applies pedagogical rules.
For example:
low organization + low cohesion = discourse organization problem
no revision = weak self-regulated revision
feedback viewed but no revision = low feedback uptake
help-seeking after difficulty = adaptive self-regulation
This step is the core interpretive layer of the study. It connects analytics to pedagogy.
Why this step follows thresholds
Because rules require classified conditions. A rule cannot work on raw numbers alone; it needs thresholded evidence.
Why this step matters
Because this is where data become
instructional meaning
. The system now knows not only that a variable is weak, but
what kind of educational problem it represents
.
6. Apply analytical models to enrich the diagnosis
After the rule-based layer, you use analytical models to deepen the diagnosis.
6.1 Clustering
Clustering groups students into learner profiles such as:
strategic writers
engaged but developing writers
effortful but struggling writers
disengaged learners
This tells you
what type of learner
the student is.
6.2 Random Forest
Random Forest predicts writing performance or improvement and identifies the strongest variables influencing progress.
This tells you
how likely the student is to improve
and
which factors matter most
.
6.3 Bayesian modelling
Bayesian inference estimates latent competencies such as:
argumentation competence
cohesion competence
linguistic control
self-regulated revision
This tells you
what underlying competences are probably weak or developing
.
Why this step follows rule-based interpretation
Because the rule layer already provides a pedagogical diagnosis, and the AI layer refines and extends that diagnosis.
Why this step matters
Because it adds three forms of insight:
learner profiling
prediction
probabilistic competence inference
This makes the adaptive decision more precise and individualized.
7. Synthesize all outputs into one learner diagnosis
At this stage, the system combines:
threshold results
rule-based interpretations
learner profile from clustering
predicted performance from Random Forest
competence probabilities from Bayesian inference
This produces a
complete adaptive learner profile
.
Why this step follows the modelling stage
Because the outputs of thresholds, rules, and AI all need to be combined before any final feedback decision can be made.
Why this step matters
Because adaptive feedback should not be based on one variable only. It must be based on a
converging evidence profile
.
This is where your study becomes truly integrative.
8. Make the final pedagogical decision
Now the system — under your supervision — decides:
what kind of feedback is needed
what the feedback should focus on
how explicit the feedback should be
what onsite instructional support should accompany it
For example:
a disengaged learner may need motivational and planning support
an effortful but struggling learner may need strategic writing instruction
a feedback-responsive learner may need deeper feedforward guidance
Why this step follows diagnosis synthesis
Because you cannot choose the right feedback until you understand the learner’s state in a complete way.
Why this step matters
Because this is the stage where analytics become
pedagogical action
.
Without this step, the study remains descriptive rather than adaptive.
9. Deliver adaptive feedback and onsite intervention
The student receives:
written feedback selected by the system
teacher-supervised pedagogical support onsite
This may include:
rubric walkthroughs
claim–evidence–explanation modelling
paragraph restructuring
grammar mini-lessons
feedback decoding sessions
clarification conferences
Why this step follows the final pedagogical decision
Because once the need is identified, the learner must receive a response tailored to that need.
Why this step matters
Because the purpose of the study is not simply diagnosis. It is
intervention
.
This is where the adaptive system actually attempts to improve learning.
10. Student revises the draft
The learner then acts on the feedback and revises the text.
This stage is extremely important because it shows whether feedback was understood, accepted, and transformed into action.
Why this step follows feedback delivery
Because the effect of adaptive pedagogy can only be observed if the learner responds to it.
Why this step matters
Because revision is the point where
learning becomes visible
. It reveals whether the adaptive support changed the learner’s writing process or product.
11. Measure growth across drafts
Now you compare:
first draft
revised draft
You analyse:
rubric score gain
stronger argumentation
improved cohesion
better lexical control
reduced error density
better feedback uptake
Why this step follows revision
Because growth can only be measured after revision has occurred.
Why this step matters
Because this is the stage that proves whether the adaptive system is effective.
This is also where your intervention becomes empirically meaningful.
12. Interpret the findings at the study level
Finally, you interpret the results across all students and cases.
You ask:
Which learner profiles were most common?
Which variables predicted improvement most strongly?
Which competencies remained most difficult?
Did adaptive feedback produce measurable improvement?
How did process, product, and help-seeking interact?
Why this step follows growth measurement
Because only after you observe change can you make conclusions about the success of the study.
Why this step matters
Because this is where the thesis answers its research questions and contributes to the field.
The logical connection between all steps
The strongest way to understand the study is as a
cause-and-response chain
:
Step 1 gives the instructional context
Without a clear task and rubric, the study has no valid foundation.
Step 2 produces the evidence
Students must interact with the task so that process and product data exist.
Step 3 transforms evidence into variables
Without indicators, the evidence remains unusable.
Step 4 classifies the variables
Without thresholds, the variables remain descriptive only.
Step 5 interprets them pedagogically
Without rules, thresholds cannot lead to educational meaning.
Step 6 enriches the diagnosis analytically
Without profiling, prediction, and competence inference, adaptation remains too simple.
Step 7 synthesizes the learner state
Without integration, the system remains fragmented.
Step 8 selects the appropriate pedagogical response
Without a final decision stage, there is no adaptation.
Step 9 delivers the intervention
Without feedback and teaching action, the system has no educational effect.
Step 10 allows the student to respond
Without revision, there is no evidence of uptake.
Step 11 measures improvement
Without growth analysis, the effectiveness of the system cannot be demonstrated.
Step 12 generates the thesis contribution
Without interpretation, the study cannot answer its research questions.
The whole study in one sequence
Task design and rubric

Student writes in Moodle

Process and product data are collected

↓
Personalized feedback and onsite intervention are selected

↓

↓
Findings are interpreted for the thesis

The strength of your study is that it is
not a loose combination of methods
. It is a
coherent adaptive architecture
in which each stage solves a different problem and prepares the ground for the next one.
Data collection

3.X Analytical Sequence of the Study
The present study is structured as an integrated adaptive assessment cycle in which each stage produces the evidence required for the next stage. The sequence begins with the design of the writing task and assessment framework, proceeds through the collection and interpretation of process and product data, and culminates in adaptive feedback, revision, and growth analysis. This organization is methodologically important because it ensures that academic writing assessment, learning analytics, adaptive pedagogy, and AI-supported modelling function as interdependent layers of one coherent research architecture rather than as parallel components.
First, the study establishes the instructional and assessment context through the design of the academic writing task, the Moodle learning environment, the analytic rubric, and the supporting instructional resources. This stage is foundational because all subsequent data and interpretations depend on clearly defined task expectations and performance criteria. In the absence of a well-specified task and rubric, neither writing quality nor learner behaviour can be interpreted validly.
Second, students complete the writing task in Moodle, thereby generating two complementary streams of evidence: process data and product data. Process data include behavioural indicators such as assignment views, rubric consultation, resource access, time-on-task, revision frequency, feedback views, and help-seeking messages. Product data include the written text itself, rubric scores, and computational textual indicators such as word count, lexical diversity, cohesion index, and error density. This stage is crucial because the study is premised on the view that academic writing development must be understood through both how students write and what they write.
Third, raw data are transformed into analyzable indicators. Moodle traces are converted into measures of engagement, planning behaviour, revision behaviour, and feedback uptake, while the essays are transformed into rubric-based and text-analytic indicators of writing quality. This step is necessary because raw logs and unprocessed texts do not yet constitute meaningful analytical variables. Indicator construction therefore serves as the bridge between data collection and interpretation.
Fourth, the indicators are classified through threshold specification. Each variable is categorized into pedagogically meaningful bands such as low, moderate, or strong. For example, low word count may signal underdevelopment, while zero revision may indicate weak self-regulated revision. These thresholds are defined on the basis of task expectations, analytic rubric logic, writing theory, self-regulated learning theory, and pilot calibration. Their function is to convert numerical values into diagnostic signals that can support subsequent interpretation.
Fifth, the thresholded indicators are interpreted through a rule-based pedagogical layer. At this stage, predefined decision rules translate indicator patterns into educationally meaningful diagnoses. For instance, weak organization combined with low cohesion is interpreted as a discourse organization problem, while feedback viewed without revision is interpreted as low feedback uptake. This stage is central to the logic of the study because it is where analytics become pedagogy. The rule layer ensures that the system remains transparent, interpretable, and theoretically grounded.
Sixth, the study applies analytical modelling to enrich the diagnosis. Learner clustering is used to identify profiles such as strategic writers, engaged but developing writers, or disengaged learners. Random Forest modelling is used to predict writing performance or improvement and to identify the most influential predictors of writing growth. Bayesian modelling is then used to estimate latent competencies, such as argumentation, cohesion, linguistic control, and self-regulated revision, on the basis of observed behavioural and textual evidence. These models do not replace the pedagogical rule layer; rather, they refine and extend it by adding profiling, prediction, and probabilistic competence inference.
Seventh, the outputs of thresholds, rule-based interpretation, clustering, Random Forest prediction, and Bayesian inference are synthesized into a unified learner diagnosis. This synthesis stage is methodologically important because adaptive feedback should not be based on a single indicator in isolation. Instead, the system integrates multiple forms of evidence in order to generate a comprehensive learner profile.
Eighth, the study moves to the final pedagogical decision stage. On the basis of the integrated learner diagnosis, the system selects the most appropriate feedback type, degree of explicitness, and onsite instructional intervention. At this point, the AI-supported system functions as a decision-support mechanism, while the teacher retains final pedagogical oversight. This preserves the instructional integrity of the intervention and ensures that adaptive feedback remains context-sensitive and educationally valid.
Ninth, personalized adaptive feedback is delivered to the student, both through the system and through onsite instructional support. Depending on the diagnosed need, the intervention may include argument scaffolding, cohesion support, corrective feedback, metacognitive prompts, feedback-decoding support, or dialogic clarification. This stage is essential because the purpose of the study is not merely to diagnose student performance, but to intervene in ways that improve it.
Tenth, the student revises the text in response to the adaptive support. The revised draft provides new evidence of whether the feedback was understood, internalized, and acted upon. Revision therefore serves as the observable site of feedback uptake and self-regulated writing behaviour.
Eleventh, growth is measured across drafts. Improvement is evaluated by comparing the first and revised drafts using rubric scores, text-analytic indicators, and alignment with feedback targets. This stage demonstrates whether adaptive support produced measurable development in argumentation, cohesion, linguistic accuracy, lexical control, or overall writing quality.
Finally, the study interprets these results at the overall research level. The analysis examines how behavioural engagement relates to writing quality, how learner profiles differ, which variables most strongly predict improvement, and to what extent adaptive feedback contributes to writing development. In this way, the thesis is able to show not only that learning analytics can diagnose academic writing behaviour, but also that analytics-informed adaptive assessment can support measurable writing growth.
This sequence is methodologically strong because each stage is functionally linked to the next. Task design enables valid data generation; data generation enables indicator construction; indicators enable thresholding; thresholds enable rule-based interpretation; interpretation is enriched by analytical modelling; modelling informs feedback selection; feedback leads to revision; and revision allows growth to be measured. The study therefore operates as a continuous adaptive cycle in which evidence is transformed into diagnosis, diagnosis into intervention, and intervention into observable learning development.

relationship is linear
Example:

Use
Spearman correlation
when:
variables are ordinal
data are skewed
sample is small
you are unsure about normality
Example:
rubric views ↔ argument score
help-seeking ↔ score gain
My recommendation for your thesis
Because educational data are often not normally distributed, and some variables are count-based or ordinal,
Spearman correlation is the safer starting point
.
So if you want one defensible answer:
Use
Spearman
first for most process–product relationships.

Step 5. Understand the output
Suppose you get this:

correlation

0.35

How to interpret it
Correlation coefficient
This shows
direction and strength
.
positive = as one goes up, the other goes up
negative = as one goes up, the other goes down
Rough interpretation
0.10–0.29 = weak
0.30–0.49 = moderate
0.50+ = strong
So:
0.62
= strong positive relationship
0.48
= moderate positive relationship
p-value
This shows whether the relationship is statistically significant.
Usually:
p &lt; .05
= statistically significant
So:
revision frequency ↔ score gain is significant
feedback views ↔ score gain is significant
help-seeking ↔ lexical score is not yet significant in this example
Step 6. Write it academically
Here is how you report one result:
A statistically significant positive correlation was found between revision frequency and writing improvement (Spearman’s rho = .62, p = .003), suggesting that students who revised more frequently tended to achieve greater gains across drafts.
Another example:
Feedback views were moderately associated with score gain (rho = .48, p = .021), indicating that engagement with instructor feedback may support writing development.

Step 9. Decide how the system uses the correlation results
Important point:
Correlation results usually do
not directly select feedback for one student
.
Instead, they serve
three larger functions
.
Function 1: Research-level validation
They help prove in your thesis that:
revision is related to improvement
feedback uptake is related to quality gain
help-seeking is related to lexical or discourse development
Function 2: Feature selection
They help you decide which variables should enter:
clustering
Random Forest
Bayesian modelling
Function 3: Pedagogical justification
They support your rule system.
For example:
if revision frequency is strongly related to score gain, then rules about revision become more defensible
if resource access is related to cohesion, then discourse-support resources are pedagogically justified
So correlation strengthens the logic of your whole system.

, not 50.

time_on_task ↔ total_score
That is already enough to build a strong results section.

Which learning behaviours move together with better writing outcomes?
It does
not

It shows
association
.
That is enough for your study at this stage.

explicit_strategic_instruction
support strategy, not motivation
strong profile needing extension
metacognitive_extension
extend and challenge high performer
low predicted improvement
intensive_support_plan
strong structured support
medium/high predicted improvement
targeted_development_feedback
focus support narrowly on growth area
medium competence probability
argument_refinement
/
discourse_refinement
/
language_refinement
stabilize a developing competence
low SRL revision probability
metacognitive_training
teach planning-monitoring-evaluation explicitly
These template families align well with the feedback literature’s distinction between task, process, and self-regulation levels of feedback.

7. The principle you should keep in the thesis
The system should be described like this:
raw data
are the evidence
analysis
transforms evidence into indicators
AI outputs
express learner-state diagnoses
rules
interpret those states pedagogically
feedback templates
operationalize the intervention
So the feedback is not chosen directly from raw data, and not directly from AI alone. It is chosen from the
AI output interpreted through pedagogical rules
.
That is the strongest version.
8. One thesis-ready summary paragraph
The proposed system operates through a layered diagnostic architecture in which process and product data are first transformed into measurable indicators, then analysed through descriptive, correlational, predictive, and probabilistic methods in order to generate AI learner-state outputs. These outputs include planning risk, engagement risk, argumentation state, discourse state, revision depth, feedback uptake, learner profile, predicted improvement, and latent competence probabilities. The rule-based pedagogical layer then interprets these learner states and maps them onto predefined feedback template families, ensuring that adaptive feedback remains individualized, transparent, and theoretically grounded in writing assessment, self-regulated learning, learning analytics, and feedback literacy.
